import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "google/electra-small-discriminator"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 511
batch_size = 1

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

For attention with causal masking: - Q, K, V each of shape [seq, hidden] = [511, 256] - We process [num_heads=4, head_dim=64] - For each head h and query position i: - scores[i, j] = dot(Q[i, h*64:(h+1)*64], K[j, h*64:(h+1)*64]) / sqrt(64) - For causal LM: j <= i (causal mask) - softmax over valid j's - out[i, h*64:(h+1)*64] = sum_j attn_score[j] * V[j, h*64:(h+1)*64] - Then output projection: [seq, hidden] @ [hidden, hidden] For the attention inside CUDA: - We can have each warp process one head for one query position - Or each block process a tile of queries With seq=511, num_heads=4: - Total "query-head" pairs = 511 * 4 = 2044 - If each warp (32 threads) handles one query-head pair: - Threads can parallelize over head_dim (64) for computing dot products - Need to compute dot product with all 511 keys - 32 threads can compute partial sums in parallel - For V weighted sum, similar approach Actually, with 32 threads and head_dim=64, each thread computes 2 elements for each key dot product. Then we reduce within the warp. This is very manageable. Let me sketch the attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_attention(...) { // Each block handles a tile of queries, say 16 or 32 queries // Or each warp handles 1 query-head pair int query_idx = blockIdx.x * queries_per_block + threadIdx.y; // y dimension for query in tile int head_idx = blockIdx.y; // or blockIdx.z // Load Q for this query and head into registers (64 floats) float q[64]; for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < 64; d += 32) { q[d/32] = Q[query_idx * hidden + head_idx * 64 + d]; } float max_score = -INFINITY; float sum_exp = 0.0f; float out[64] = {0}; // First pass: compute max score for numerical stability for (int key_tile = 0; key_tile < seq_len; key_tile += tile_size) { // Load K tile into shared memory __shared__ float k_tile[tile_size][64]; // ... __syncthreads(); for (int j = 0; j < tile_size && (key_tile + j) <= query_idx; j++) { // compute dot product q . k[key_tile + j] float score = 0; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 64/32; d++) { score += q[d] * k_tile[j][threadIdx.x + d*32]; } // warp reduce // ... score *= rsqrtf(64.0f); max_score = max(max_score, score); } } // Second pass: compute softmax and weighted sum // ... } ``` Wait, for causal attention, j <= query_idx (if 0-indexed and causal means token i can only attend to tokens 0..i). For the transformer kernel, let me design it more carefully. **Transformer kernel design:** The kernel processes the entire sequence (batch=1, seq=511) through all 12 layers. For each layer, the computation is: 1. Load input activations (per token: 256 floats) 2. Compute Q, K, V projections (256 -> 768 per token) 3. Compute attention (for each head, each query: dot products with keys, softmax, weighted sum of values) 4. Output projection (256 -> 256 per token) 5. Residual add + LayerNorm 6. FFN: Linear1 (256 -> 1024 per token) + GELU + Linear2 (1024 -> 256 per token) 7. Residual add + LayerNorm For memory, the total weights per layer: - QKV: 256 * 768 = 196,608 floats (768KB) - Out proj: 256 * 256 = 65,536 floats (256KB) - FFN1: 256 * 1024 = 262,144 floats (1MB) - FFN2: 1024 * 256 = 262,144 floats (1MB) - LayerNorm params: negligible Total: ~3MB per layer, 36MB for all layers. To process all of this in a single kernel, we can have blocks process tiles of the sequence. **Block assignment:** - Grid dimension X: Number of token tiles. With tile_size=64, we have ceil(511/64) = 8 tiles. - Grid dimension Y: could be 1, or layers if we want parallelism (but layers are sequential). - So grid = [8, 1, 1]. **Block size:** - 256 or 512 threads. **Shared memory per block:** For processing 64 tokens through one layer: - Input buffer: 64 * 256 * 4 = 64KB - QKV buffer: 64 * 768 * 4 = 192KB - Attention output: 64 * 256 * 4 = 64KB - FFN intermediate: 64 * 1024 * 4 = 256KB - Plus weight tiles: maybe 256KB Total would exceed 227KB. So we need to be more careful. We can: 1. Reuse buffers. QKV can be computed and consumed immediately. FFN intermediate can be computed and consumed. 2. Use smaller tiles. For a block processing 16 tokens: - Input: 16 * 256 * 4 = 16KB - QKV: 16 * 768 * 4 = 48KB - Attn out: 16 * 256 * 4 = 16KB - FFN intermediate: 16 * 1024 * 4 = 64KB - K and V cache for all tokens: 511 * 256 * 2 * 4 = 1MB (must be in global memory) Total active shmem: ~144KB. Plus weight tiles. Manageable. But 16 tokens is a small tile. With 511 tokens, we'd have ~32 blocks. On H100 with many SMs, this is fine. Actually, let me think about this differently. What if I process one token at a time per thread? Or use a warps/blocks design where each block processes one token through all layers? If each block handles one token, then: - Block 0: process token 0 through all 12 layers - Block 1: process token 1 through all 12 layers - ... - Block 510: process token 510 through all 12 layers But attention requires communication between tokens! Block 510 needs to know the K and V values for tokens 0..510. So this doesn't work unless we store intermediate activations for all tokens in global memory after each layer. **Two-phase approach:** - Store all token activations for each layer in global memory - For each layer, use a grid that processes all tokens This is essentially what PyTorch does, but we fuse all ops within each "superlayer" (attention + MLP) into one kernel. **Superlayer kernel design:** Input: [seq, hidden] Output: [seq, hidden] Operations inside kernel for one layer: 1. For each token: LN -> QKV projection 2. For each head and each query: attention computation using all K, V 3. For each token: output projection -> residual -> LN -> FFN1 -> GELU -> FFN2 -> residual -> LN For storing K, V globally: we need [seq, hidden] each = 512KB. This is fine. To minimize memory traffic, we can: - Load input tokens into registers/shmem - Compute Q, K, V - Write K, V to global memory (coalesced) - Do attention by reading K, V from global memory - Compute the rest and write output This is a single kernel per layer, plus we need an embedding kernel and an LM head kernel. But to maximize fusion, we can merge: - Embedding + first few layers? Not really, they depend on previous layer outputs. - But we can compute embedding and feed directly into layer 0 if we write them to global memory first. Let's design the layer kernel in detail. **LayerKernel:** Inputs: `input` [seq, hidden], various weights for the layer Output: `output` [seq, hidden] ```cuda __global__ void layer_kernel(float* input, float* output, LayerWeights weights, int seq_len, int hidden) { // Each block processes a tile of sequence positions int token_start = blockIdx.x * TILE_SIZE; int token_end = min(token_start + TILE_SIZE, seq_len); int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp = tid / 32; extern __shared__ float shmem[]; // Phase 1: Compute Q, K, V for all tokens in this tile // Each warp handles one token's QKV projection for (int token = token_start + warp; token < token_end; token += warps_per_block) { // Load input token into registers/shmem // LayerNorm // QKV projection: we can do this with each thread computing a chunk of the 768 outputs // ... // Write K, V to global memory } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Attention for each query token in this tile for (int token = token_start; token < token_end; token++) { // All warps in block cooperate to compute attention for this token // Each warp handles one head for (int head = warp; head < num_heads; head += warps_per_block) { // Load Q for this token and head // Iterate over KV tiles // Compute scores, softmax, output // Write to temp buffer } } __syncthreads(); // Phase 3: Output projection, residual, FFN for (int token = token_start + warp; token < token_end; token += warps_per_block) { // Output projection // Residual add // LayerNorm // FFN1 // GELU // FFN2 // Residual add // LayerNorm // Write to output } } ``` This is complex but doable. However, writing the QKV projection and FFN as efficient matmuls in CUDA is still tricky. Actually, for hidden=256 and intermediate=1024, the matmuls are small. We can write very simple but correct matmuls: - Each thread computes one output element - For QKV: 768 outputs per token. With 256 threads, each thread computes 3 outputs. - For FFN1: 1024 outputs per token. With 256 threads, each thread computes 4 outputs. This simple approach might actually be sufficient and is much easier to write correctly! For small M=1 and N<=1024, the naive approach (each thread does a dot product) is fine because: - The weight reads are coalesced (threads in warp read adjacent columns) - The input reads are broadcast (all threads read the same input row) - This is essentially outer-product or GEMV style For a batch of tokens processed by a block, each warp can handle one token, with 32 threads each computing some output columns. For 256 threads = 8 warps, we process 8 tokens simultaneously for the projections. Then for attention, we can have each warp handle one head of one token. Each requires 256 * 256 = 256KB of weights. Still too big for shared memory if we also need input buffer. We can tile further: process 64 outputs at a time. Weight tile: 256 * 64 = 64KB. Total = 1M flops. - At 67 TFLOPS, 1M flops = 15 nanoseconds... but memory limited - Actually, reading 64KB weights + 64KB inputs = 128KB. At 3TB/s = 0.04 microseconds. - Wait, for 64 tokens, each needs to read 256 inputs = 64KB total. But if weights are in shared memory, all threads read from shmem. The total memory traffic is just loading weights to shmem once. Actually, in this tiled approach, the FLOP intensity is: - Work: 64 tokens * 64 outputs * 256 MACs = 1M MACs - Memory: 64KB weights + 64KB inputs (but inputs might be in registers or read once) = 128KB - Arithmetic intensity: 1M / 128K = 8 FLOPs/byte - H100 bandwidth: 3.35 TB/s - At bandwidth bound: 128KB / 3.35TB/s = 0.038 microseconds, but we can do 1M FLOPs in that time at AI=8? Actually 3.35TB/s * 8 = 26.8 TFLOPS, which is less than 67 TFLOPS. Actually, we can fuse even more if we pre-load weights and do everything in shared memory. **Let me try to implement a fully fused layer kernel.** I'll write a CUDA kernel that processes a single transformer layer: - Input: [seq, hidden] - Output: [seq, hidden] - All weights and biases passed as pointers - The kernel handles LN -> QKV -> Attention -> OutProj -> Residual -> LN -> FFN1 -> GELU -> FFN2 -> Residual -> LN For the matmuls, I'll use a simple but correct approach: - Each block handles a chunk of the sequence (e.g., 64 tokens) - Within the block, each warp handles one token's linear operations - For the attention, all warps cooperate Wait, if each warp handles one token for linear ops, we have 32 threads per token. For QKV output of 768 elements, each thread computes 24 output columns. This means each thread does 24 dot products of length 256? That's 24 * 256 = 6144 MACs. With 32 threads in warp, total parallelism is good. But the weight access pattern: thread 0 reads weight columns 0, 32, 64, ..., thread 1 reads columns 1, 33, 65, ... For good coalescing, adjacent threads should read adjacent addresses. So we should have thread i compute output columns i, i+32, i+64, etc. Then thread i reads weight[i], weight[i+32], etc. But these are strided by 32 * hidden = 8192 bytes apart. Not ideal coalescing for global memory. Better: thread i computes a contiguous chunk of outputs. If each thread computes 24 outputs (768/32=24), then thread 0 computes outputs 0..23, thread 1 computes 24..47, etc. To compute output j, the thread needs weight[j * hidden + k] for k in 0..255. So thread 0 reads weight[0..255], weight[256..511], etc. Thread 1 reads weight[6144..6399], etc. Global memory reads are coalesced if the entire warp reads together - so warp reads 32*24*256 = 196K floats in a somewhat coalesced pattern, but with bank conflicts. Actually, a better approach for GEMV in CUDA is to reduce across the K dimension rather than parallelize over N. **GEMV approach for QKV:** - Output: 768 elements - We have 32 threads in a warp - Each thread computes a partial sum for multiple output elements - Actually, we can parallelize over N=768 with 32 threads, so each thread handles 24 outputs. For computing output[j]: `out[j] = sum_k input[k] * W[j*256 + k]` With 32 threads, thread t computes outputs t, t+32, t+64, ..., t+32*23. Each thread reads input[0..255] (same for all threads within a warp - broadcast from L1/L2 cache) and then reads its own weight columns strided by 32 * hidden. This creates a strided memory access pattern for weights. For full coalescing, threads should read adjacent floats. So a better pattern is: - Thread t computes outputs starting at t * (768/32) = t * 24. - Thread 0 computes out[0..23], thread 1 computes out[24..47], etc. - For each k from 0 to 255: - All threads read input[k] (broadcast/cached) - Thread t reads W[t*24*256 + k], W[t*24*256 + 256 + k], ... for 24 elements - These reads are strided by 256 within thread t, and adjacent threads read adjacent starting positions. Wait, if thread t reads W[t*6144 + k], W[t*6144 + 256 + k], ..., then adjacent threads read adjacent floats only at the first read: thread 0 reads W[0], thread 1 reads W[6144], etc. These are 6144 * 4 = 24KB apart - terrible coalescing! To fix this, we should tile the computation. Process the output in tiles, where within a tile, adjacent threads read adjacent weights. For example, process outputs in tiles of 32. In each tile, thread t computes output `base + t` where `base` is the tile start. Then for each k: - All threads read input[k] - Thread t reads W[(base+t)*256 + k] - These are adjacent memory locations! W[base*256 + k + t*256 + ...] - Wait, W[(base+t)*256 + k] = W[base*256 + t*256 + k]. For fixed k, adjacent t reads locations 256 apart. That's 1024 bytes apart. Still not great. Actually for a row-major weight matrix where W[j, k] is at W[j * hidden + k], accessing W[j, k] for varying j and fixed k gives stride = hidden = 256 floats = 1024 bytes. This is exactly one L1 cache line for floats? Actually a cache line is 128 bytes, so 1024 bytes = 8 cache lines apart. Not coalesced. For coalesced access, we want adjacent threads to read adjacent bytes. So if thread t reads W[j, k+t], then accesses are adjacent. This means each thread handles one output j and processes k in chunks of 32. So for GEMV: - Each warp handles 32 output elements (one per thread). - For each k_tile of 32 elements: - Load 32 input elements (or just input[k_tile + lane] for thread lane) - Wait, for GEMV, all outputs use the same input vector. - Each thread stores its input vector element? No, each output element j is `sum_k input[k] * W[j, k]`. - To parallelize over j, each thread handles one j. To read weights coalesced, for each k, thread lane reads W[j, k] but j is different per thread. Hmm, actually for weights W[j, k] where j varies across the warp and k is the same, the stride is hidden = 1024 bytes. This is NOT coalesced. To get coalescing, we need to access W[j, k] where k varies across the warp. So we should parallelize over k and reduce across the warp. **GEMV via reduction over K:** - Each thread handles a subset of K for multiple outputs? No. - Each warp handles one output j. - Thread lane handles input[k_lane] and W[j, k_lane], W[j, k_lane+32], etc. - This way, in the first step, thread 0 reads W[j, 0], thread 1 reads W[j, 1], etc. These ARE coalesced! - Each thread computes partial sum over its assigned K elements. - At the end, warp shuffle reduce to get the full sum. For 32 threads and K=256, each thread handles 8 K values. - Time: 8 iterations, each with one coalesced weight read + one input read (the input can be kept in registers or shmem). - At the end, warp shuffle reduce across 32 threads. This is a very efficient GEMV pattern! It's often called "reduction GEMV." For QKV with N=768 outputs: - We need 768 warps, or if we have multiple warps per block, 768 / warps_per_block blocks. - With 8 warps per block = 256 threads, each block handles 8 outputs. - Number of blocks = 768 / 8 = 96 blocks for a single token. - For 511 tokens, we'd need 96 * 511 = 48,768 blocks. This is a lot of blocks but manageable on H100 (132 SMs * 32 blocks/SM = 4224 concurrent blocks max... actually blocks are limited by thread count and registers). But with 256 threads per block, each SM can run at most a few blocks concurrently. 48K blocks means many waves. At 132 SMs, 48K / 132 = 364 waves per SM. At ~1 microsecond per wave (for compute-bound), that's 364 microseconds. But memory bandwidth might make it slower. Actually, this is getting into very complex analysis. Let me write the attention kernel as a separate function. **Attention kernel design:** Input: Q [seq, hidden], K [seq, hidden], V [seq, hidden] Output: O [seq, hidden] Parameters: num_heads, head_dim, scale For query position i, head h: 1. Read Q[i, h*head_dim : (h+1)*head_dim] into registers 2. For key position j = 0 .. seq-1: a. Read K[j, h*head_dim : (h+1)*head_dim] b. Compute dot product c. Scale d. (Apply causal mask if needed: j <= i) e. Update max and running softmax sum 3. For key position j = 0 .. seq-1: a. Compute softmax score b. Read V[j, ...] c. Accumulate weighted sum 4. Write to O[i, h*head_dim : (h+1)*head_dim] Then O is multiplied by output projection weight. To fuse output projection, after step 4, we could immediately do the linear projection. But we need the full O for all heads. So we can compute O for all heads of a query position, then do the projection. Grid: [seq, 1, 1] - one block per query position (511 blocks) or [seq / tile, 1, 1] Block: [num_heads, head_dim, 1] = [4, 64, 1] = 256 threads. Perfect! Wait, 256 threads per block is nice. But we need to compute softmax over seq=511 elements. How do threads cooperate? With 256 threads = 4 heads * 64 dims, all threads in the block work on ONE query position. But each head's attention is independent. For head h, the 64 threads can help compute the attention. For head h: - Thread d (0..63) helps compute: - Dot products for all 511 keys (each dot product is 64-dim) - Weighted sum for all 511 keys We can have all 64 threads cooperate on one dot product at a time: - For key j: - Thread d reads K[j, h, d] - Thread d reads Q[i, h, d] - Thread d computes product - Warp shuffle reduce sum across 64 threads (2 warps) - But warp shuffle within a warp of 32 is native. Across 2 warps requires shared memory. Alternative: each thread computes partial sums for multiple keys: - Thread d handles keys j where j % 64 == d - For each assigned key, compute dot product (single-threaded, since only one thread per key) - Then all threads write scores to shared memory - Do softmax over 511 elements using parallel reduction - Then each thread computes weighted sum for its assigned keys - Reduce across threads With 64 threads and 511 keys: - Each thread handles 8 keys (512/64 = 8). - Thread 0 handles keys 0..7, thread 1 handles 8..15, etc. - For each assigned key, thread computes dot product (64 ops) and writes to shared memory. - After all keys are computed, 64 threads do parallel softmax on 512 elements in shmem. - Then each thread computes weighted sum for 8 keys and accumulates. - Finally, write 64-dim output. Wait, this is messy. Let me simplify. With 64 threads working on one head: **Phase 1: Compute all scores** - Use a loop over key tiles (e.g., tile_size = 64) - For each tile of 64 keys: - All 64 threads load Q[i, h, d] (64 floats) - Load K tile [64, 64] into shared memory (coalesced: thread d loads K[tile_start + d, 0..63]?) - Actually, for coalescing, we want thread d to load contiguous memory. So K should be stored as [seq, num_heads, head_dim]. Thread d loads K[tile_start + t, h, d] for a specific t? - For tile of keys, we can have each thread load one key's vector: thread t loads K[tile_start + t, h, 0..63]. Each thread does a strided read. With head_dim = 64, each thread reads 64 floats. Not great coalescing. This is getting complicated. Let me just use a simpler design for the attention: one block per query-head pair, with 32 or 64 threads, where each thread computes the dot product and weighted sum for a subset of keys. Block: [32, 1, 1] For query position i, head h: - Thread t handles keys j where j starts at t and increments by 32 - For each key j: - Compute dot product Q[i] · K[j] (thread performs all 64 multiplications and reductions internally? No, we want parallelism) Actually, for dot product of two 64-dim vectors, having 32 threads cooperate is good. But we have 511 dot products to compute. With 32 threads, each thread computes ~16 dot products. For each dot product, a single thread can do it serially: 64 multiply-adds. For 16 dot products = 1024 MACs. Then all threads write their scores to shmem. Then we do softmax over 511 elements with 32 threads. Then each thread computes weighted sum for its 16 keys. This design is simple and probably fast enough: 1. Load Q[i, h, :] into registers (all 32 threads load the same data, or we distribute it) 2. For j = t; j < seq; j += 32: a. Load K[j, h, :] into registers b. Compute score (64 MACs) c. Store score in shared memory 3. Synchronize 4. Compute softmax over all scores in shared memory (32 elements per thread, parallel max, parallel sum) 5. For j = t; j < seq; j += 32: a. Load V[j, h, :] b. Multiply by softmax score and accumulate into output registers 6. Write output to global memory For 511 keys and 32 threads: each thread handles 16 keys. Score computation: 16 * 64 = 1024 MACs per thread. Weighted sum: 16 * 64 = 1024 MACs per thread. Total 2048 MACs per thread = 65K MACs per block. At 67 TFLOPS, a block takes ~1 microsecond. But the memory reads are: - Q: 64 floats = 256 bytes - K: 16 * 64 = 1024 floats per thread = 4KB per block. But coalesced across 32 threads? No, each thread reads its own K[j, :] independently, strided by 32 in j. - V: similar to K. If K is stored as [seq, num_heads, head_dim], then K[j, h, :] and K[j+1, h, :] are not adjacent. They're separated by head_dim floats if num_heads * head_dim is the inner dimension? Actually in PyTorch, typical storage is: - Linear output for QKV is [seq, 3 * hidden]. Or if split: Q is [seq, hidden]. - If [seq, hidden] and hidden = num_heads * head_dim = 256, then the layout is [seq, 256]. - For head h, elements are at [seq, h*64 : (h+1)*64]. - For K[j], the elements are at j*256 + h*64 + d. So for thread t reading K[t + k*32], memory accesses are to (t + k*32)*256 + h*64 + d. For fixed d and varying t, these are 256*4 = 1024 bytes apart. For a full warp reading together, the addresses are 0, 32*1024, 64*1024, ... for consecutive threads. That's not coalesced at all! This is a fundamental problem. To get coalesced access, adjacent threads should read adjacent memory locations. For attention, to read K[j, h, :] for multiple j in a coalesced way, we could have: - Warp reads K[j, h, 0..31] where j is the same for all threads. Then thread d reads element d. These ARE coalesced! - Then warp reads K[j, h, 32..63] So instead of each thread reading one full key, all threads read one key together! But then we need 511 such reads for one query. With 32 threads cooperating on one key at a time, we can compute the dot product in parallel and reduce. **Revised attention kernel (coalesced):** One block per query-head pair. Block size = 64 threads. All 64 threads cooperate on each key j: - Thread d reads K[j, h, d] if d < 64. Perfect coalescing! - Compute Q[i, h, d] * K[j, h, d] for each thread - Warp shuffle reduce sum to get score for key j - Thread 0 stores score in shmem - After all keys, do softmax - Then for each key j, all threads read V[j, h, d], multiply by score, accumulate output With 64 threads and one key at a time: - 511 keys * 2 reads (K and V) * 64 elements = 65K reads. - Plus shuffle/reduce overhead. - Total ~100K operations. With 64 threads doing this in parallel, it's about 1500 iterations per thread. At ~10-20 cycles per iteration... with H100 clock at ~2GHz, ~15K cycles = ~7.5 microseconds per block. For 511 queries * 4 heads = 2044 blocks. At 132 SMs on H100, can run ~16 blocks per SM concurrently (if 64 threads), so 2112 blocks concurrently. So we need about 1 wave. Total time ~7.5us * (2044 / 2112) ~ few microseconds. But wait, we're reading K and V for all 511 keys from global memory for each query. With 2044 queries, total K/V reads = 2044 * 511 * 256 * 4 bytes = ~1GB of memory traffic! At 3TB/s, that's ~0.33ms. But PyTorch probably does something similar. The difference is PyTorch might cuBLAS the Q @ K^T and use optimized kernels. For our custom kernel, 0.33ms for attention per layer is actually quite slow if there are 12 layers = 4ms total just for attention. But PyTorch's reference time might be similar or slower due to overhead. Actually, for a small model like this, PyTorch might take 2-5ms total. So 4ms for attention is not competitive. We need to use shared memory to cache K and V across queries! Since K and V are the same for all queries, if multiple query blocks (for different i) read the same K and V, we can cache them in L2. But with 511 query blocks all reading K and V for 511 keys, there's a total of 512KB per head. With 4 heads = 2MB. The H100 L2 cache is 50MB, so K and V for all heads can fit in L2 cache! Once loaded by the first query blocks, subsequent queries will get L2 hits. So in practice, total K/V bandwidth is ~one full read per layer per head, not per query! Because after the first wave of queries loads K and V into L2, subsequent queries benefit. Wait, but each query-head block is independent. If they all run concurrently, they all miss L2 simultaneously. But as blocks complete and new ones start, K and V might still be in L2. For 2044 blocks reading 2MB of K+V, if they all load simultaneously, total memory traffic is 2044 * 512KB = 1GB. But with L2 caching, maybe much less. Realistically, L2 cache can hold the full K+V for a head. But with concurrent execution, many blocks load simultaneously. This analysis is getting too detailed. For attention, each block needs to read K and V from all other positions. These K and V values are computed by other blocks in previous layers. This creates a dependency issue: block 0 computes K and V for its token at layer 0. Block 1 also needs to compute K and V for its token at layer 0. These are independent. But for layer 1, block 0 needs to read K and V from all tokens computed at layer 0. So we need a global synchronization between layers. Global synchronization between all blocks in a kernel launch is only possible using multiple kernel launches or grid synchronization (cooperative groups). Cooperative groups require specific launch conditions and are complex. So we need one kernel launch per layer. This brings us back to ~12 launches for the transformer layers. In ELECTRA: ```python class ElectraAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.self = ElectraSelfAttention(config) self.output = ElectraSelfOutput(config) self.pruned_heads = set() class ElectraSelfAttention(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.query = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size) self.key = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size) self.value = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.attention_probs_dropout_prob) ``` Where is LayerNorm? In `ElectraSelfOutput`: ```python class ElectraSelfOutput(nn.Module): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__() self.dense = nn.Linear(config.hidden_size, config.hidden_size) self.LayerNorm = nn.LayerNorm(config.hidden_size, eps=config.layer_norm_eps) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(config.hidden_dropout_prob) ``` And its forward is: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, input_tensor): hidden_states = self.dense(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.LayerNorm(hidden_states + input_tensor) return hidden_states ``` So it's: 1. It's at the END of each block (post-LN architecture like original Transformer, not pre-LN). So the flow is: ``` input -> self_attn -> self_output_dense -> dropout -> add(input) -> LN -> intermediate_dense -> GELU -> output_dense -> dropout -> add(prev) -> LN ``` I was wrong earlier about pre-LN. Let me verify: in `ElectraLayer`: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): self_attention_outputs = self.attention(hidden_states, ...) attention_output = self_attention_outputs[0] outputs = self_attention_outputs[1:] intermediate_output = self.intermediate(attention_output) layer_output = self.output(intermediate_output, attention_output) outputs = (layer_output,) + outputs return outputs ``` And `ElectraAttention.forward`: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, ...): self_outputs = self.self(hidden_states, ...) attention_output = self.output(self_outputs[0], hidden_states) outputs = (attention_output,) + self_outputs[1:] return outputs ``` Yes, this is post-LN: - Self-attention on hidden_states - self_output: dense(dropout(attn)) + hidden_states + LN - intermediate: dense(gelu(attention_output)) - output: dense(dropout(intermediate)) + attention_output + LN OK so the architecture is confirmed. Better to separate: 1. **Phase 1 (all blocks)**: Each block computes Q, K, V for its assigned token. Write K and V to global memory. 2. **Syncthreads across grid** for the layer: not possible in one kernel. So if Q, K, V are computed per block, and all blocks write K, V to global memory, we DON'T need a separate sync because global memory writes are visible. But there's no guarantee ALL blocks have finished writing K and V before some block starts reading them. In practice, with 511 blocks, some blocks might start the attention phase and read K, V from other blocks before those blocks have finished computing their K, V. This is a race condition! To avoid this, we need either: A. Two kernel launches: one for QKV projection, one for attention + FFN B. Use grid synchronization (cooperative groups `this_grid().sync()`) Option B requires `cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel` which we can't easily use with `load_inline` unless we write a custom launcher. Option A is simpler: 2 kernels per layer. - Kernel 1 (`qkv_proj_kernel`): LN + QKV projection. Each block handles one token. Writes Q, K, V to global memory. - Kernel 2 (`attention_ffn_kernel`): Attention + output projection + residual + LN + FFN. Each block handles one token. Reads K, V from global memory (now fully written). Writes output. Total: 24 kernel launches for transformer layers + 1 for embeddings + 1 for LM head = 26 launches. ELECTRA doesn't do LN before QKV. It does input -> QKV directly. Let me re-read the ELECTRA layer: ``` self_attention_outputs = self.attention(hidden_states) attention_output = self_attention_outputs[0] intermediate_output = self.intermediate(attention_output) layer_output = self.output(intermediate_output, attention_output) ``` And `ElectraAttention`: ``` self_outputs = self.self(hidden_states, ...) attention_output = self.output(self_outputs[0], hidden_states) ``` So attention is on `hidden_states` directly, no pre-LN. The output of attention goes through `self.output`, which does dense + dropout + add(residual) + LN. So for our kernel: 1. `qkv_proj_kernel`: input -> Q, K, V (no LN!) 2. `attention_kernel`: Q, K, V -> attention_output (including output projection and softmax) 3. `ffn_kernel`: attention_output -> FFN -> output (including residual, LN, etc.) Wait, but the attention kernel reads K and V from all tokens. If we use one kernel per token for QKV, and then one kernel per token for attention, the attention kernel will read K and V from global memory (which were written by the QKV kernel). This is fine with 2 separate kernel launches. Actually, we can merge kernel 1 and 2! We can have: - Launch 1: `qkv_proj_kernel`. Each block writes Q, K, V for its token. - This ensures all Q, K, V are in global memory. - Launch 2: `attention_ffn_kernel`. Each block reads Q for its token and K/V for all tokens. Computes attention, output proj, residual, LN, FFN, residual, LN. That's 2 launches per layer. For 12 layers = 24 + embedding + LM head = 26 total. Even better: if we do the math right, we might not even need a separate attention kernel if we can compute QKV, then __syncthreads within the same kernel. But since blocks are independent, we need the global sync. Two kernel launches is the way. **Kernel 1: `qkv_proj_kernel`** Grid: [ceil(seq / tokens_per_block), 1, 1] Block: [256, 1, 1] Each block handles `tokens_per_block` tokens. For token_idx in assigned tokens: 1. Load input[token_idx, :] into registers (256 floats) 2. Compute Q[token_idx, :] = input @ W_Q + b_Q - 256 threads, each computes one output element. But Q has 256 elements. If block handles multiple tokens, we need more parallelism. Actually, if block handles 1 token, 256 threads for 256 outputs is 1:1. Perfect! But then for Q+K+V = 768 outputs, we'd need 768 threads per token or process them sequentially. Wait, with 256 threads per block and 1 token per block: - Warp 0 (32 threads): compute Q[0..255] - Wait, 32 threads for 256 outputs = 8 outputs per thread - Each thread computes 8 dot products of length 256 - Warp 1: compute K[0..255] - Warp 2: compute V[0..255] That's only 3 warps = 96 threads used. Rest idle. Total blocks: 511. With 96 active threads per block, occupancy is low. But it's still 511 concurrent blocks across 132 SMs = ~4 blocks per SM. Fine. Actually, we could have 512 threads per block covering 2 tokens. Each token gets 256 threads. But 512 threads might limit occupancy. Hmm, for CUDA on H100, occupancy is rarely the bottleneck for small models. Memory latency is more important. Let me go with: - Block: 256 threads - Each block handles 1 token - Thread i computes Q[i], K[i], V[i] for i in 0..255 - Wait, that's 256 outputs each for Q, K, V. With 256 threads, each thread computes: - Q[i] = dot(input[0..255], W_Q[i*256 + 0..255]) - K[i] = dot(input[0..255], W_K[i*256 + 0..255]) - V[i] = dot(input[0..255], W_V[i*256 + 0..255]) - So each thread does 3 dot products of length 256 = 768 MACs. - At the same time, all threads read input[0..255] concurrently (broadcast) and weights strided by 256. The weight access pattern: thread i reads W_Q[i*256 + k] for k=0..255. Threads 0,1,2 read W_Q[0..255], W_Q[256..511], W_Q[512..767]. These are not adjacent memory locations accessed by adjacent threads. The stride is 1024 bytes. This gives poor coalescing. To fix coalescing, we should have threads in a warp read adjacent addresses. So for computing output Q[j], the threads should read W_Q[j*256 + k] for varying j? No, that doesn't help. Actually, for a GEMV where output is 256 elements and input is 256 elements, and we have 256 threads: - If thread j computes output j, then for each k, thread j reads W[j, k]. - If we process k in a loop, in iteration k, thread j reads W[j, k]. - Adjacent threads read W[0, k], W[1, k], W[2, k], etc. If W is row-major, these are separated by K=256 floats = 1024 bytes. If W is transposed (stored as [K, N]), then W[k, j] is adjacent for adjacent j. Then in iteration k, adjacent threads read adjacent floats W[k, 0], W[k, 1], etc. This IS coalesced! So if we transpose the weights to [K, N] format (e.g., [256, 256] instead of [256, 256] but with layout where column j is stored contiguously), then adjacent threads reading varying j for fixed k will be coalesced. In CUDA, the standard approach for GEMV is to store weights in column-major or transposed format. I'll pre-transpose all weight matrices before passing them to the kernel. **Transposed weight layout:** - W_Q: [hidden, hidden] -> store as [hidden, hidden] but in memory, column j is contiguous? - Actually, if we want thread j to read W_transposed[k, j] in coalesced manner, we can transpose the PyTorch tensor: `W_Q_t = W_Q.t().contiguous()` So W_Q_t has shape [hidden, hidden] but W_Q_t[k, j] = W_Q[j, k]. Then for iteration k: - Thread j reads W_Q_t[k, j] - Adjacent j's read adjacent memory -> coalesced! Yes! This is the standard trick. So in Python: ```python self.W_Q = [layer.attention.self.query.weight.t().contiguous() for layer in ...] ``` And in CUDA: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float x = input[k]; for (int j = threadIdx.x; j < hidden; j += blockDim.x) { Q[j] += x * W_Q_t[k * hidden + j]; } } ``` With blockDim.x = 256, each thread handles one j. In iteration k, thread j reads W_Q_t[k*hidden + j]. Adjacent threads read adjacent floats. Coalesced! For Q, K, V, we have three weight matrices. We can compute them all in one kernel. **`qkv_proj_kernel` specification:** ```cuda __global__ void qkv_proj_kernel( const float* input, float* q, float* k, float* v, const float* W_Q_t, const float* b_Q, const float* W_K_t, const float* b_K, const float* W_V_t, const float* b_V, int seq_len, int hidden ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; // Load input token float x[256]; // or use shared memory for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { x[i] = input[token_idx * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { float sum = b_Q[i]; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { sum += x[k] * W_Q_t[k * hidden + i]; } q[token_idx * hidden + i] = sum; } // Compute K // Compute V } ``` Wait, storing `x[256]` in registers for each thread is too much (256 registers per thread, 256 threads = 65K registers per block, exceeding the 64K limit per SM!). Actually, each thread only stores its own `x`. No, in this design, each thread needs the full input vector to compute its output. So each thread would need 256 floats = 256 registers. For 256 threads: 256 * 256 = 65,536 registers per block. H100 has 64K registers per SM. This exceeds the limit! If we use 64 threads per block: - 64 threads * 256 registers = 16,384 registers. Good. - But then each block handles fewer outputs concurrently. Alternative: use shared memory for the input vector. - Input vector: 256 floats = 1KB. One block loads this into shared memory once. - Each thread reads from shared memory. **Revised kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void qkv_proj_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; __shared__ float x_shared[256]; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; // Cooperative load of input for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { x_shared[i] = input[token_idx * hidden + i]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q, K, V for (int i = tid; i < hidden; i += blockDim.x) { float q_sum = b_Q[i]; float k_sum = b_K[i]; float v_sum = b_V[i]; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float val = x_shared[k]; q_sum += val * W_Q_t[k * hidden + i]; k_sum += val * W_K_t[k * hidden + i]; v_sum += val * W_V_t[k * hidden + i]; } q[token_idx * hidden + i] = q_sum; k[token_idx * hidden + i] = k_sum; v[token_idx * hidden + i] = v_sum; } } ``` With blockDim.x = 256: - 256 threads, each computes one output element for Q, K, V. - Shared memory: 1KB for input. - Register usage: each thread needs a few registers for loop variables and accumulators. ~16 registers per thread. Total ~4K. Very low. - Each block handles 1 token. Total blocks = 511. Weight reads: each block reads the full W_Q_t, W_K_t, W_V_t (3 * 256 * 256 * 4 = 768KB). For 511 blocks: 393MB total. But with L2 caching, after first ~50 blocks load the weights, subsequent blocks get L2 hits. Total HBM traffic: ~768KB. Time per block: ~256 iterations of inner loop, each with 3 multiply-adds. 768 FLOPs per thread. With 256 threads: 196K FLOPs per block. At 67 TFLOPS = 3 microseconds per block. But memory bound. With 768KB weight reads and L2 miss for first few blocks: maybe 10-20 microseconds amortized per block. Total: ~5-10ms for QKV projection across 511 tokens. Wait, that seems high. Actually, with blocks running concurrently across 132 SMs, if each SM runs 4 blocks, 528 blocks run concurrently. They all read weights simultaneously, so L2 doesn't help until later waves. First wave: 528 blocks * 768KB = 405MB. At 3TB/s = 135 microseconds. But L2 bandwidth is 20TB/s, so once in L2, it's fast. For 511 tokens, ~1 wave. Total time: ~135 microseconds for weights + some compute. That's not bad! For the attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* attn_output, const float* W_O_t, const float* b_O, int seq_len, int hidden, int num_heads, int head_dim ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head_idx = threadIdx.y; // if using 2D block ``` Actually, let me use a 2D block for attention: - Block: [head_dim, num_heads] = [64, 4] = 256 threads - Grid: [seq_len, 1, 1] Each block handles one query position. Within the block, each thread handles one dimension of one head. For head h and dim d: 1. Load q[token_idx, h*64 + d] 2. Compute attention score with all keys: - For key j = 0..seq_len-1: - score_j += q_d * k[j, h*64 + d] (but we need dot product over all d!) This requires reduction across head_dim. For 64 threads per head, we can do warp shuffle reduce. But if we process all keys in a loop, with 64 threads doing warp shuffle for each key: - For key j: - Thread d reads k[j, h*64 + d] - Warp computes dot product using shuffle reduce - Store score in shared memory or registers Wait, with 4 heads and 64 threads each = 256 threads, each head gets a warp (2 warps actually, since warp size is 32). Hmm, 64 threads = 2 warps. Warp shuffle only works within a warp. For 64 threads, we need two warp shuffles or shared memory reduce. Actually, for head_dim=64, we can have 32 threads per head and process 2 elements per thread. - Block: [32, 4] = 128 threads - Thread (d, h) handles q[token, h, d*2] and q[token, h, d*2+1] For dot product with key j: - Thread reads k[j, h, d*2] and k[j, h, d*2+1] - Computes q0*k0 + q1*k1 = partial - Warp shuffle (sum across 32 threads) to get full score for key j Then the 32 threads in warp h all know the score for key j. They can compute softmax collectively. For weighted sum with value j: - Warps load v[j, h, :] and multiply by score - Each thread accumulates partial sum for its 2 dimensions This design uses 128 threads (4 warps), each handling 2 elements. Total blocks: 511. Each block: 1. For j = 0..510: a. Read K[j, h, :] for all h. Each warp reads 64 floats = 256 bytes. 4 warps = 1KB per key. b. Compute score for each head via shuffle reduce c. Store scores in shared memory (511 floats per head? Too big! 4 * 511 * 4 = 8KB. That's fine for shared memory.) Wait, 511 floats per head * 4 heads = 2044 floats = 8KB. Fits in shared memory. 2. Softmax over 511 scores per head: - Parallel max and sum over 511 elements with 32 threads - Can be done with reduction 3. For j = 0..510: a. Read score from shmem b. Read V[j, h, :] c. Accumulate weighted sum 4. Output projection: - Result per head is 64-dim. Concatenate to 256-dim. - Linear(256, 256) per token: again a GEMV Let me refine this. ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head_idx = threadIdx.y; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; extern __shared__ float shmem[]; float* scores = shmem; // [num_heads, seq_len] but too big: 4 * 512 = 2K floats // Actually, we can have scores per head computed and processed in a streaming fashion, // without storing all scores. But we need all scores for softmax. // Load Q for this token and head float q[2]; q[0] = q_input[token_idx * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2]; q[1] = q_input[token_idx * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2 + 1]; float max_score = -INFINITY; // First pass: compute max score #pragma unroll 4 for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { float k0 = k_input[j * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2]; float k1 = k_input[j * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2 + 1]; float dot = q[0]*k0 + q[1]*k1; // warp shuffle sum dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 16); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 8); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 4); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 2); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 1); if (lane_id == 0) { dot *= scale; scores[head_idx * ((seq_len + 31) / 32 * 32) + j] = dot; // store for later max_score = fmaxf(max_score, dot); } } __syncthreads(); // Broadcast max_score within warp // Actually, need max over all keys within the head // lane 0 has max_score. Broadcast to all lanes. max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_score, 0); // Second pass: compute sum of exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; if (lane_id == 0) { for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { float score = scores[...]; sum_exp += expf(score - max_score); } } sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_exp, 0); // Third pass: compute weighted sum float out0 = 0, out1 = 0; for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { float score; if (lane_id == 0) score = expf(scores[...] - max_score) / sum_exp; score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, score, 0); float v0 = v_input[j * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2]; float v1 = v_input[j * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2 + 1]; out0 += score * v0; out1 += score * v1; } // Write attention output attn_temp[token_idx * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2] = out0; attn_temp[token_idx * hidden + head_idx * head_dim + lane_id * 2 + 1] = out1; __syncthreads(); // Now do output projection // Wait, all threads need to be done writing attn_temp // Then we can do a GEMV: attn_temp[token] @ W_O_t + b_O // Load attention output into shared memory or registers // Actually, with 128 threads and 256 outputs, each thread can compute 2 outputs // ... } ``` This is getting very complex. I'll assume `.eval()` mode and skip dropout in my kernel. **Now, Layer Normalization details:** PyTorch `nn.LayerNorm(hidden_size, eps=1e-12)` computes: ``` mean = x.mean(dim=-1, keepdim=True) var = x.var(dim=-1, unbiased=False, keepdim=True) x_norm = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) output = x_norm * gamma + beta ``` IMPORTANT: `unbiased=False` (population variance, N not N-1). This is crucial for matching PyTorch exactly. In CUDA: ```cuda float mean = 0; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) mean += x[i]; mean /= hidden; float var = 0; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= hidden; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { out[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * gamma[i] + beta[i]; } ``` **GELU details:** PyTorch `nn.GELU()` uses the approximate formula: ``` 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) ``` Wait, actually PyTorch's GELU `torch.nn.functional.gelu` default uses the exact formulation: ``` 0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2))) ``` But in transformers library, when config says `hidden_act = "gelu"`, what does `ACT2FN["gelu"]` return? In `transformers.activations`: ```python class GELUActivation(nn.Module): def forward(self, input): return F.gelu(input) ``` And `torch.nn.functional.gelu` uses `torch._C._nn.gelu` which calls `at::gelu`. In PyTorch, `at::gelu` uses the exact formulation by default (approximate=False): ``` 0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2))) ``` Actually, let me verify. In PyTorch >= 1.12, `F.gelu` has an `approximate` parameter that defaults to `'none'`, which means exact `erf`. Other options are `'tanh'` for the tanh approximation. So for exact matching, I should use: ```cuda float gelu(float x) { return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * M_SQRT1_2)); } ``` Where `M_SQRT1_2` is 1/sqrt(2). **Softmax details:** Computed over the last dimension (seq_len). For numerical stability, subtract max before exp. **Now let me write the actual code.** I'll write a Python file with `ModelNew` that: 1.
