This means each thread does 4 dot products of length 256. - For output GEMV: 256 outputs. 256 threads, each computes 1 output. Each thread does 1 dot product of length 1024. This is feasible. Let me write the exact CUDA code now. I'll keep it as clean as possible. For the embeddings kernel: ```cuda __global__ void electra_embeddings_kernel( const int64_t* input_ids, float* output, const float* word_embed, const float* pos_embed, const float* type_embed, const float* embed_ln_gamma, const float* embed_ln_beta, const float* proj_weight_t, const float* proj_bias, int seq_len, int vocab_size, int embed_dim, int hidden_dim, int max_pos ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; int64_t token_id = input_ids[token_idx]; // Load embeddings float x[128]; // embed_dim for (int i = 0; i < embed_dim; i++) { x[i] = word_embed[token_id * embed_dim + i] + pos_embed[token_idx * embed_dim + i] + type_embed[i]; // token_type = 0 } // LayerNorm float mean = 0; for (int i = 0; i < embed_dim; i++) mean += x[i]; mean /= embed_dim; float var = 0; for (int i = 0; i < embed_dim; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= embed_dim; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-12f); // Apply LN and project to hidden_dim // For projection: [embed_dim] @ [embed_dim, hidden_dim] -> [hidden_dim] // We need hidden_dim = 256 outputs, and we have only 1 thread per token here. // This won't parallelize well. } ``` Wait, with 1 thread per token, the projection is serial. For 128 threads per block and 4 blocks, we'd have 512 threads total. Each thread handles 1 token but computing 256 outputs serially is 256 * 128 MACs = 32K MACs per thread, which is slow. Better to have 256 threads per token for the projection. But then we need multiple blocks for the 511 tokens. Let me restructure: each block handles 1 token with 256 threads. - Grid: [seq_len, 1, 1] = [511, 1, 1] - Block: [256, 1, 1] For the embedding lookup: - Thread 0-127: load word, position, type embedding elements 0-127 - All threads: compute layer norm - All threads: compute projection output[0-255] This needs shared memory for the embedding vector. ```cuda __global__ void electra_embeddings_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; __shared__ float x_shared[128]; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; int64_t token_id = input_ids[token_idx]; // Cooperative load of embedding if (tid < embed_dim) { x_shared[tid] = word_embed[token_id * embed_dim + tid] + pos_embed[token_idx * embed_dim + tid] + type_embed[tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute mean and var float mean = 0; if (tid == 0) { for (int i = 0; i < embed_dim; i++) mean += x_shared[i]; mean /= embed_dim; x_shared[0] = mean; // reuse shmem } __syncthreads(); mean = x_shared[0]; float var = 0; if (tid == 0) { for (int i = 0; i < embed_dim; i++) { float diff = x_shared[i] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= embed_dim; x_shared[1] = var; } __syncthreads(); var = x_shared[1]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-12f); // Apply LN if (tid < embed_dim) { x_shared[tid] = (x_shared[tid] - mean) * inv_std * embed_ln_gamma[tid] + embed_ln_beta[tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Projection: x_shared [embed_dim] -> output [hidden_dim] // Each thread computes one output element if (tid < hidden_dim) { float sum = proj_bias[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < embed_dim; i++) { sum += x_shared[i] * proj_weight_t[i * hidden_dim + tid]; } output[token_idx * hidden_dim + tid] = sum; } } ``` This looks good. **QKV Projection Kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void electra_qkv_kernel( const float* input, float* q, float* k, float* v, const float* W_Q_t, const float* b_Q, const float* W_K_t, const float* b_K, const float* W_V_t, const float* b_V, int seq_len, int hidden ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; __shared__ float x_shared[256]; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; // Load input if (tid < hidden) { x_shared[tid] = input[token_idx * hidden + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute Q, K, V // Each thread computes one output element for each of Q, K, V if (tid < hidden) { float q_sum = b_Q[tid]; float k_sum = b_K[tid]; float v_sum = b_V[tid]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { float val = x_shared[i]; q_sum += val * W_Q_t[i * hidden + tid]; k_sum += val * W_K_t[i * hidden + tid]; v_sum += val * W_V_t[i * hidden + tid]; } q[token_idx * hidden + tid] = q_sum; k[token_idx * hidden + tid] = k_sum; v[token_idx * hidden + tid] = v_sum; } } ``` **Attention Kernel:** This is the most complex. Let me write it carefully. ```cuda __global__ void electra_attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* residual_input, float* output, const float* W_O_t, const float* b_O, const float* ln_gamma, const float* ln_beta, int seq_len, int hidden, int num_heads, int head_dim, float scale ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; // We'll use 64 threads per head, process 2 elements per thread // Total: 4 * 64 = 256 threads. But we can also just use 256 threads // with thread tid handling head (tid / 64), dim (tid % 64) int head = tid / head_dim; int d = tid % head_dim; if (tid >= num_heads * head_dim) return; // if we have extra threads // Actually for 256 threads, tid 0-255, head = tid/64 = 0..3, d = tid%64 = 0..63. Perfect. // Load q for this token, head, dim float q_val = q[token_idx * hidden + head * head_dim + d]; // Compute attention scores for this head // We need to store all scores for softmax // 511 scores per head. For 4 heads, 2044 floats = 8KB shared memory __shared__ float scores[4][512]; // padded to 512 // Compute scores for all keys #pragma unroll 8 for (int j = 0; j < seq_len; j++) { // dot product: read k[j, head, d] for this thread float k_val = k[j * hidden + head * head_dim + d]; float dot = q_val * k_val; // Warp reduce sum for this head unsigned int mask = 0xFFFFFFFF; for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(mask, dot, offset); } if (d == 0) { // lane 0 in each warp... wait, with 64 threads per head = 2 warps // d == 0 is in warp 0 for each head // But we need the sum across BOTH warps // Warp 0 lanes 0-31 for head h, d=0..31 // Warp 1 lanes 0-31 for head h, d=32..63 // shfl_down only works within warp // Need to pass across warps using shared memory } } } ``` Hmm, with 64 threads per head and only warp shuffle within 32 threads, reducing across 64 threads requires shared memory. Let me redesign the block organization. Block: [32, 4] = 128 threads Grid: [seq_len, 1, 1] Each block handles one token. Within the block: - Thread (lane, head) where lane in 0..31, head in 0..3 - Each thread handles 2 dimensions: d = lane and d = lane + 32 - For head `head`, thread computes partial dot products for dims lane and lane+32 ```cuda __global__ void electra_attention_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x; int head = threadIdx.y; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; // Load q float q0 = q[token_idx * hidden + head * head_dim + lane]; float q1 = q[token_idx * hidden + head * head_dim + lane + 32]; __shared__ float scores[4][512]; __shared__ float max_score_shared[4]; __shared__ float sum_exp_shared[4]; // Compute scores float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k0 = k[j * hidden + head * head_dim + lane]; float k1 = k[j * hidden + head * head_dim + lane + 32]; float dot = q0 * k0 + q1 * k1; // Reduce within warp dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 16); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 8); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 4); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 2); dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 1); if (lane == 0) { dot *= scale; scores[head][j] = dot; local_max = fmaxf(local_max, dot); } } // But we only computed scores for j where j % 32 == lane, not all j! // This stores scores分散 across threads. // Actually, after the warp shuffle, thread 0 knows dot. But only for j = lane, lane+32, ... // So lane 0 stores for j=0,32,64,...; lane 1 for j=1,33,65,... // After all j are processed, we need to find global max for this head // We can use shared memory for a second reduction if (lane == 0) { max_score_shared[head] = local_max; } __syncthreads(); // But wait, local_max is only the max over j=lane,lane+32,... and lane == 0 means j=0,32,64,... // Each warp lane computed max for its own j's. We need max across all lanes. // This is getting very messy. } ``` Let me use a different approach. Each block of 4 warps handles one query token. We use 4 warps, one per head. Each warp (32 threads) handles one head. For head h, warp w = h: - Thread lane in warp handles dim d = lane. Only 32 dims, but head_dim = 64. - So each thread handles 2 dims: d = lane and d = lane + 32. For score computation: - Warp computes dot product for each key j. - Thread lane loads k[j, h, lane] and k[j, h, lane+32] - Computes q[lane]*k[j,lane] + q[lane+32]*k[j,lane+32] - Warp shfl reduce to get full dot product - Thread 0 stores score and updates local max Then after all scores are stored, warp 0 computes softmax over all scores and the weighted sum. Wait, with 4 warps in a block (one per head), one warp can handle one head. All 32 threads in the warp cooperate to compute the attention for that head. Within warp h: 1. Thread lane loads q[lane] and q[lane+32] (2 dims per thread) 2. For each key j: a. Thread lane loads k[j, lane] and k[j, lane+32] b. Computes dot = q0*k0 + q1*k1 c. Warp shuffle reduce (sum across 32 threads) -> score d. Thread 0 stores score[j] in shared memory 3. After all scores: a. All 32 threads find max score via parallel reduction b. All 32 threads compute sum of exp(score - max) via parallel reduction c. All 32 threads compute softmax scores 4. For each key j: a. Thread lane loads v[j, lane] and v[j, lane+32] b. Thread lane accumulates score[j] * v[j, lane] and score[j] * v[j, lane+32] This is clean! One warp per head, 4 warps per block, block size = 128. But we also need to write output to global memory. The attention result per head is 64-dim. Then we need to apply the output projection. For output projection: [seq, 256] @ [256, 256]. We can have the 4 warps compute this for the token. - Total outputs: 256. 128 threads. Each thread computes 2 outputs. - Thread lane in warp h computes outputs head*64 + lane and head*64 + lane + 32. Wait, if we have 128 threads, each can compute 2 outputs. 128 * 2 = 256. Perfect. But the attention output is in registers. We need to write it to shared memory first, then compute the projection. Actually, we can compute the projection directly using the attention output registers: - attn_out[lane] and attn_out[lane+32] are in registers for warp h - To compute output projection output[j] = sum_i attn_out[i] * W_O_t[i, j] - Each thread would need all 256 attn_out values to compute its output elements. That requires a broadcast. Better to write attention output to shared memory, then do the projection. Let's finalize the attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void electra_attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* residual_input, float* output, const float* W_O_t, const float* b_O, const float* ln_gamma, const float* ln_beta, int seq_len, int hidden, int num_heads, int head_dim, float scale ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int warp_id = threadIdx.y; // 0..3 (one per head) if (token_idx >= seq_len || warp_id >= num_heads) return; int head = warp_id; int head_offset = head * head_dim; // Shared memory: scores for all keys (one per head) // With 4 heads and seq_len=511, we need 4 * 512 floats = 8KB __shared__ float scores_shared[4][512]; // Also shared memory for attn output before projection __shared__ float attn_out_shared[256]; // Load Q float q0 = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float q1 = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; // Compute scores for all keys for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32) { float k0 = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float k1 = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; float dot = q0 * k0 + q1 * k1; // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane == 0) { scores_shared[head][key_idx] = dot * scale; } } __syncwarp(); // Find max score for this head float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int i = lane; i < seq_len; i += 32) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, scores_shared[head][i]); } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } float max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, 0); // Compute sum of exp float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = lane; i < seq_len; i += 32) { local_sum += expf(scores_shared[head][i] - max_score); } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } float sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Compute weighted sum float out0 = 0.0f; float out1 = 0.0f; for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32) { float score = expf(scores_shared[head][key_idx] - max_score) * inv_sum; float v0 = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float v1 = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; out0 += score * v0; out1 += score * v1; } // Reduce across warp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { out0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, out0, offset); out1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, out1, offset); } // Write attention output to shared memory if (lane == 0) { attn_out_shared[head_offset + 0] = out0; // Wait, warp can only write once // Actually each thread in the warp should write its own dim } // No, the weighted sum was reduced, so only lane 0 has the full sum for dims lane and lane+32. // Wait! For key_idx reduction, we reduced across the key dimension, but each lane was computing // for different key positions. After __shfl_down, lane 0 has the total sum for out0 and out1. // But out0 corresponds to dim `lane`, not dim 0! ``` I see the issue. Let me be more careful. For the weighted sum: - Thread `lane` computes: - `out0 = sum_{key_idx = lane, lane+32, ...} score[key_idx] * v[key_idx, lane]` - `out1 = sum_{key_idx = lane, lane+32, ...} score[key_idx] * v[key_idx, lane+32]` After warp shuffle reduce: - `out0_total = sum_{lane=0..31} out0_lane` = sum over all keys of score * v[:, lane] - `out1_total = sum_{lane=0..31} out1_lane` = sum over all keys of score * v[:, lane+32] But after `__shfl_down`, only `lane=0` has `out0_total` and `out1_total`. And `out0_total` is the sum for dimension `lane` of the calling thread? No! Actually, each thread `lane` computes `out0` for dimension `lane`. After warp reduce, `lane=0` has `sum_lane out0_lane`. But `out0_lane` is the partial sum for dimension `lane`. So the total `sum_lane out0_lane` = `sum_lane sum_keys score * v[:, lane]`. This is a sum across all dimensions and all keys, which is NOT what we want. We want the sum over keys for EACH dimension separately. The warp shuffle sum gives us the sum of all threads' `out0` values, but each thread's `out0` is for a different dimension! So we'd be summing dimension 0 + dimension 1 + ... + dimension 31. That's wrong! We need each thread to keep its own `out0` (for its own dimension) and NOT reduce across threads for this part. Wait, let me re-think. `out0` computed by thread `lane` is: `out0_lane = sum_{key_idx in lane's keys} score[key_idx] * v[key_idx, lane]` After the loop, `out0_lane` is the partial sum for dimension `lane` over a subset of keys. We need to sum these partial sums ACROSS threads to get the full sum for dimension `lane`: `full_out[lane] = sum_{thread t} out0_t` where `out0_t` is thread t's partial sum for dimension t? No! Actually, thread t computed `out0 = sum_{keys assigned to t} score * v[:, t]`. So `out0` IS for dimension t. If we do warp shuffle of `out0`, we get: `sum_t out0_t = sum_t sum_{keys of t} score * v[:, t]` = `sum_t sum_{keys of t} score * v[keys, t]` = sum over all keys of score * v for all dimensions mixed together... Wait, no! The keys are different for each thread! Thread 0 summed over keys 0, 32, 64, ... for dimension 0. Thread 1 summed over keys 1, 33, 65, ... for dimension 1. `sum_t out0_t` = `sum_t sum_{k=t+32*n} score[k] * v[k, t]` This doesn't equal any single dimension's full sum. It's a scramble of different dimensions and keys! So we CANNOT reduce `out0` across threads with shuffle. Instead, we need to transpose the data or use shared memory to accumulate per-dimension sums. Here's the fix: ```cuda __shared__ float dim_sums[2][64]; // dim_sums[0][d] for out0 (dim d), dim_sums[1][d] for out1 (dim d+32) // Each thread lane computes: float my_out0 = ...; // sum for dim = lane float my_out1 = ...; // sum for dim = lane + 32 // Write to shared memory dim_sums[0][lane] = my_out0; dim_sums[1][lane] = my_out1; __syncwarp(); // Now we need to reduce across the key dimension... but wait, each thread already // summed over all keys (in its stride). So dim_sums already contains the FULL sum // for each dimension! ``` Wait, that's right! Each thread `lane` iterates over `key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32`. This covers ALL keys because lane ranges from 0 to 31. And since we don't shuffle `out0`, no reduction is needed! Each thread computes the complete sum for its assigned dimension `lane` and `lane+32`. I was overcomplicating this. The loop: ```cuda for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32) { ... out0 += score * v0; } ``` already sums over all keys assigned to this thread lane. Since the keys partition the full set {0, 1, ..., 510} among the 32 threads, and each thread computes its own `out0` independently, the final `out0` is the full sum for dimension `lane`. No reduction needed! Similarly for `out1` and dimension `lane+32`. So after the loop, `out0` IS the correct value for dimension `lane`. No shuffle needed! Then we can simply write to shared memory: ```cuda attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane] = out0; attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane + 32] = out1; ``` And all threads participate. Perfect. Similarly for the score computation, I DID need a shuffle because each thread only computes a partial dot product (over 2 dimensions), and we need the full dot product to compute the score. That's correctly handled. So the corrected attention kernel for the weighted sum part is: ```cuda float out0 = 0.0f; float out1 = 0.0f; for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32) { float score = expf(scores_shared[head][key_idx] - max_score) * inv_sum; float v0 = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float v1 = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; out0 += score * v0; out1 += score * v1; } attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane] = out0; attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane + 32] = out1; ``` This is correct because `out0` is the complete weighted sum for dimension `lane` of this head. Now for the output projection and residual + LN: ```cuda __syncthreads(); // Now compute output projection // Each thread computes one output element // 128 threads, each computes 2 outputs // Thread (lane, head) computes outputs: head_offset + lane and head_offset + lane + 32 // Actually, we have 128 threads organized as [32, 4]. // But for projection, we can have each thread compute 2 outputs. float out_val_0 = b_O[head_offset + lane]; float out_val_1 = b_O[head_offset + lane + 32]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { out_val_0 += attn_out_shared[i] * W_O_t[i * hidden + head_offset + lane]; out_val_1 += attn_out_shared[i] * W_O_t[i * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; } // Add residual float res0 = out_val_0 + residual_input[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float res1 = out_val_1 + residual_input[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; // Wait, to do LayerNorm, we need the full 256-dim vector in shared memory // But we only have partial computation. So we need to write back to shared memory first. ``` For the output projection and LN, we need better coordination. Let me write the complete attention kernel: ```cuda __global__ void electra_attention_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int lane = threadIdx.x; int head = threadIdx.y; if (token_idx >= seq_len || head >= num_heads) return; __shared__ float scores_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float attn_out_shared[256]; __shared__ float result_shared[256]; // after projection and residual int head_offset = head * head_dim; // Load Q float q0 = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float q1 = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; // Compute attention scores for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32) { float k0 = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float k1 = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; float dot = q0 * k0 + q1 * k1; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane == 0) { scores_shared[head][key_idx] = dot * scale; } } __syncwarp(); // Find max score float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int i = lane; i < seq_len; i += 32) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, scores_shared[head][i]); } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } float max_score = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, 0); // Compute sum exp float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = lane; i < seq_len; i += 32) { local_sum += expf(scores_shared[head][i] - max_score); } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } float sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Weighted sum float out0 = 0.0f; float out1 = 0.0f; for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += 32) { float score = expf(scores_shared[head][key_idx] - max_score) * inv_sum; float v0 = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float v1 = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; out0 += score * v0; out1 += score * v1; } attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane] = out0; attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane + 32] = out1; __syncthreads(); // Output projection for this token // Each thread computes 2 outputs float proj0 = b_O[head_offset + lane]; float proj1 = b_O[head_offset + lane + 32]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { float val = attn_out_shared[i]; proj0 += val * W_O_t[i * hidden + head_offset + lane]; proj1 += val * W_O_t[i * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; } // Add residual proj0 += residual_input[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; proj1 += residual_input[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane + 32]; // Write to shared memory for LayerNorm result_shared[head_offset + lane] = proj0; result_shared[head_offset + lane + 32] = proj1; __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm (gamma and beta size = hidden = 256) // Compute mean and var across 256 elements if (head == 0 && lane < 16) { // use 16 threads for reduction? We need parallel reduction. // Actually, we can have all 128 threads participate } // Better: each thread computes partial sum float partial_sum = 0; for (int i = lane; i < hidden; i += 32) { partial_sum += result_shared[head_offset + i]; // Wait, this only sums within head, not across all heads } ``` For LayerNorm, we need the mean and variance across the FULL hidden dimension (256), not just one head. All 128 threads in the block need to participate. We can do: ```cuda // Compute mean float partial_sum = 0; for (int i = lane; i < hidden; i += 32) { partial_sum += result_shared[i]; } // Reduce across all 128 threads... but __shfl_down only works within a warp. // With 4 warps, we need shared memory for inter-warp reduction. ``` This is getting very complex for a single kernel. The synchronization and reductions across the full block are tricky. Alternative: Do the output projection and residual in this kernel, but skip the LayerNorm. Then do a separate tiny kernel for LayerNorm. That adds one kernel per layer, but simplifies things significantly. Or even better: have the FFN kernel start with the LayerNorm of the attention output. But ELECTRA doesn't do LN at the start of FFN. Looking at ELECTRA again: ```python intermediate_output = self.intermediate(attention_output) layer_output = self.output(intermediate_output, attention_output) ``` Where `self.output` does: dense + dropout + add(attention_output) + LN. So the FFN takes `attention_output` directly, applies intermediate + output, then LN. There's no LN between attention and FFN; the LN is applied AFTER the attention's residual addition. Actually, let me re-examine `ElectraAttention`: ```python attention_output = self.output(self_outputs[0], hidden_states) ``` And `ElectraSelfOutput`: ```python def forward(self, hidden_states, input_tensor): hidden_states = self.dense(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.dropout(hidden_states) hidden_states = self.LayerNorm(hidden_states + input_tensor) return hidden_states ``` So `hidden_states` passed to `self.output` is the self-attention output (after QKV attention). `input_tensor` is the original hidden_states. The output is: dense(dropout(attention)) + input_tensor, then LN. So `attention_output` is already layer-normalized. Then `ElectraIntermediate` takes `attention_output` and does: dense + GELU. So the FFN starts from the LN'd attention output. Then `ElectraOutput` takes the intermediate result and does: dense + dropout + add(attention_output) + LN. So the flow is: ``` hidden -> attention(QKV) -> dense -> dropout -> +hidden -> LN = attn_output attn_output -> dense(intermediate) -> GELU = intermediate_output intermediate_output -> dense(output) -> dropout -> +attn_output -> LN = layer_output ``` There is indeed no LN between attention and intermediate. The attention block ends with LN, and the FFN starts from that output directly. So for our kernels: - `attention_kernel` should output the attention_output (after the LN in `ElectraSelfOutput`) - `ffn_kernel` takes attention_output and outputs layer_output (after `ElectraOutput`) This means the attention kernel MUST include the LN at the end. And the FFN kernel MUST include the LN at the end. Given the difficulty of implementing block-wide reductions in CUDA, let me write separate kernels for the linear projections and use shared memory more carefully. Or use fewer threads per block so warp-level operations cover the whole block. Actually, with `head_dim=64` and `num_heads=4`, and `warp_size=32`, we have 4 warps = 128 threads. For reductions over 256 elements, we can tile the 256 elements into 4 chunks of 64, one per warp, and reduce within each warp. But 64 elements per warp requires 2 shuffle steps (since shuffle is within 32 threads, we'd need to first reduce within each 32-thread half-warp, then combine across halves using shared memory). This is doable but still complex. **Simplification: Use 1 warp (32 threads) per block for LN reduction.** Actually, for LN over 256 elements, we can have 256 threads (8 warps) in the block, all cooperating. But our block size is 128 for attention. What if we make the block size 256 for the attention kernel? Then: - 256 threads - 4 warps of 64 threads each? No, warp size is 32. - 8 warps of 32 threads. - We can assign warps to tasks differently. For attention with 4 heads and head_dim=64: - We could have 2 warps per head (64 threads per head). - Total: 8 warps = 256 threads. - For score computation: 64 threads per head, with warp shuffle reduce working within each group of 32, then shared memory for between the 2 warps. - For weighted sum: each of the 64 threads handles dimensions in a strided fashion, similar to before. This gives us 256 threads, which is more natural for reductions over 256 elements. Let me design with 256 threads: - Thread 0-63: head 0 - Thread 64-127: head 1 - Thread 128-191: head 2 - Thread 192-255: head 3 Within each head group of 64 threads: - Subgroup 0 (threads 0-31): dims 0-31 - Subgroup 1 (threads 32-63): dims 32-63 For dot product reduction: - Thread in subgroup computes q*d * k*d for its assigned dims. - Warp shuffle within subgroup gives partial sum. - Need to combine subgroup 0 and subgroup 1 partial sums using shared memory. - Thread 0 in each subgroup writes partial sum to shared memory. - Thread 0 of subgroup 0 reads both and sums them. For weighted sum: - Each thread handles its assigned keys and dims. - After the loop, each thread has the full sum for its dims. - Write directly to shared memory. No cross-thread reduction needed. This is manageable with 256 threads. Then for the output projection + residual + LN at the end: - All 256 threads participate. - Each thread computes 1 output of the projection. - Add residual. - Write to shared memory. - Compute mean and var with 256 threads using parallel reduction. - Apply LN. With 256 threads, we can do the parallel reduction efficiently: ```cuda // Each thread loads one element float val = result[threadIdx.x]; __shared__ float sdata[256]; sdata[threadIdx.x] = val; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for sum for (int s = 128; s > 0; s /= 2) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = sdata[0] / 256; ``` Wait, but 256 threads and reduction over 256 elements is classic. We use threadIdx.x directly. For variance: ```cuda sdata[threadIdx.x] = (val - mean) * (val - mean); __syncthreads(); // same tree reduction float var = sdata[0] / 256; ``` Then apply LN. This is clean and standard. So let me redesign the attention kernel with 256 threads: ```cuda __global__ void electra_attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* residual_input, float* output, const float* W_O_t, const float* b_O, const float* ln_gamma, const float* ln_beta, int seq_len, int hidden, int num_heads, int head_dim, float scale ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head = tid / head_dim; int lane = tid % head_dim; int subwarp = lane / 32; int sublane = lane % 32; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; __shared__ float scores_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float attn_out_shared[256]; __shared__ float result_shared[256]; __shared__ float dot_partial[4][2]; // for combining two 32-thread warp sums int head_offset = head * head_dim; // Load Q (2 elements per thread if head_dim=64 and we have 64 threads per head) // Actually, with 256 threads and head_dim=64, each thread handles one dim. float q_val = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; // Compute attention scores // Each thread handles keys key_idx = lane, lane+64, lane+128, ... for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += head_dim) { float k_val = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; // Warp shuffle within subwarp (32 threads) #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (sublane == 0) { dot_partial[head][subwarp] = dot; } __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) { float total_dot = dot_partial[head][0] + dot_partial[head][1]; scores_shared[head][key_idx] = total_dot * scale; } __syncthreads(); } // Find max score for this head float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int i = lane; i < seq_len; i += head_dim) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, scores_shared[head][i]); } // Reduce within subwarp #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } __syncthreads(); if (sublane == 0) { dot_partial[head][subwarp] = local_max; } __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) { float max_score = fmaxf(dot_partial[head][0], dot_partial[head][1]); dot_partial[head][0] = max_score; } __syncthreads(); float max_score = dot_partial[head][0]; // Compute sum of exp float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = lane; i < seq_len; i += head_dim) { local_sum += expf(scores_shared[head][i] - max_score); } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } __syncthreads(); if (sublane == 0) { dot_partial[head][subwarp] = local_sum; } __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) { dot_partial[head][0] = dot_partial[head][0] + dot_partial[head][1]; } __syncthreads(); float sum_exp = dot_partial[head][0]; float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Weighted sum float out_val = 0.0f; for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += head_dim) { float score = expf(scores_shared[head][key_idx] - max_score) * inv_sum; float v_val = v[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; out_val += score * v_val; } attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane] = out_val; __syncthreads(); // Output projection: each thread computes one output float proj = b_O[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { proj += attn_out_shared[i] * W_O_t[i * hidden + tid]; } // Add residual proj += residual_input[token_idx * hidden + tid]; result_shared[tid] = proj; __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm // Compute mean float mean_val = 0.0f; if (tid < hidden) { mean_val = result_shared[tid]; } sdata[tid] = mean_val; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 128) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 128]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 64) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 64]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 32]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 16]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 8]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 4]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 2]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 1]; } __syncthreads(); float mean = sdata[0] / hidden; // Compute variance float var_val = 0.0f; if (tid < hidden) { float diff = result_shared[tid] - mean; var_val = diff * diff; } sdata[tid] = var_val; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 128) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 128]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 64) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 64]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 32]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 16]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 8]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 4]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 2]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 1]; } __syncthreads(); float var = sdata[0] / hidden; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-12f); // Apply gamma and beta if (tid < hidden) { output[token_idx * hidden + tid] = (result_shared[tid] - mean) * inv_std * ln_gamma[tid] + ln_beta[tid]; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue in the score computation. The `key_idx` loop: ```cuda for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += head_dim) ``` where `lane = tid % head_dim` and `head_dim = 64`. If `seq_len = 511`, not all key indices from 0 to 510 are covered if `lane` ranges from 0..63. Actually, with `key_idx = lane, lane+64, lane+128, ...`, for lane 0: 0, 64, 128, 192, 256, 320, 384, 448, 512. That's 9 values, all <= 510. For lane 63: 63, 127, 191, 255, 319, 383, 447, 511. Also 9 values. All keys from 0 to 510 are covered because 511 / 64 = 7.98, so lane values up to 62 have 8 iterations and lane 63 has 8 iterations (63 + 8*64 = 575 > 510, wait 63 + 7*64 = 511 which is > 510, so actually 7 iterations...). Let me count: - lane 0: 0, 64, 128, 192, 256, 320, 384, 448, 512. But 512 >= 511 (seq_len), so stops at 448. That's 8 values. - lane 62: 62, 126, 190, 254, 318, 382, 446, 510. 8 values. - lane 63: 63, 127, 191, 255, 319, 383, 447, 511. 511 >= 511, stops at 447. 7 values. So keys are covered, and 511 is not included because `key_idx < seq_len`. This is correct. But wait, `scores_shared[head][key_idx] = total_dot * scale;` is only written when `lane == 0`. But the loop variable `key_idx` is the lane's key index, not `0`'s key index! So for head 0, when lane=0 writes scores for key_idx=0,64,128,... but lane=1 would also try to write if we didn't have the `if (lane == 0)` guard. But with `lane == 0`, only the thread with lane=0 writes, and only for its own key_idx values (0, 64, 128, ...). The threads with lane=1 don't write scores for keys 1, 65, 129, etc. This is a bug! Only keys at positions `lane=0, lane=0+64, ...` are being written. Keys at positions `lane=1, lane=1+64, ...` are NOT written to `scores_shared`! The fix: we need ALL threads to write their computed scores. But `total_dot` is only available when `lane == 0` because that's when we combine the two subwarp partial sums. Alternative design: have each subwarp compute scores for keys in its own loop, and write them to `scores_shared` independently, without combining across subwarps. Wait, each subwarp computes the dot product for its own set of keys. After warp shuffle, each thread in the subwarp knows the total dot product. So any thread in the subwarp can write it. ```cuda for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += head_dim) { float k_val = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (sublane == 0) { // thread 0 or 32 of the head group scores_shared[head][key_idx] = dot * scale; } } ``` With `lane = tid % head_dim` and `head_dim = 64`, `sublane = lane % 32`. For lane 0 and lane 32 (subwarp leaders), they write to scores_shared. For key_idx = 0: written by lane 0. For key_idx = 1: written by lane 1? No! lane 1 computes key_idx = 1, 65, 129, ... After shuffle, if `sublane == 0`, lane 0 writes key_idx=0, lane 32 writes key_idx=32. But lane 1 doesn't write anything! This is still wrong! The issue is that only sublane 0 writes, but different lanes iterate over different key_idx values. The correct fix: any thread can write after the shuffle. So remove the `if (sublane == 0)` restriction and have the thread that computed the key_idx write it: ```cuda for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += head_dim) { float k_val = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } scores_shared[head][key_idx] = dot * scale; } ``` After `__shfl_down`, ALL threads in the warp have the same `dot` value (the sum). So any of them can write it. We don't need `if (sublane == 0)`! But wait, we're iterating with `key_idx` specific to each thread. If lane 0 writes key_idx=0, lane 1 writes key_idx=1, etc., that's fine! Each thread writes its own key_idx, and after the shuffle, the `dot` in each thread is the total dot product for that `key_idx`. Yes! This works beautifully. For head h: - Thread with lane d (0..63) handles key_idx = d, d+64, d+128, ... - For each key_idx, it reads k[key_idx, h, d] and computes q[d] * k[key_idx, h, d] - After warp shuffle, the total dot product is in `dot` - Thread writes `scores_shared[head][key_idx] = dot * scale` Wait, but warp shuffle works within a warp of 32 threads. With 64 threads per head, lane 0-31 are in one warp, lane 32-63 are in another warp. The `__shfl_down_sync` with mask 0xFFFFFFFF only sums within the warp! For a 64-thread group, `__shfl_down_sync` with mask `0xFFFFFFFF` only operates within the 32 threads of the warp. So lanes 0-31 compute the dot product using their dims 0-31, and lanes 32-63 compute using dims 32-63. After shuffle, `dot` in each warp is the sum over their 32 dims, not the full 64 dims. We need to combine the two warps. That's why I had `dot_partial`. But with the fix where we remove `if (lane == 0)`: ```cuda for (int key_idx = lane; key_idx < seq_len; key_idx += head_dim) { float k_val = k[key_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (lane < 32) { scores_shared[head][key_idx] = dot * scale; } else { // lane 32-63 write to different indices? But they also need to contribute to the same key_idx! } } ``` Wait, each lane iterates over key_idx = lane, lane+64, lane+128, ... So lane 0 handles keys 0, 64, 128, ...; lane 32 handles keys 32, 96, 160, ... These are DIFFERENT keys! So both can write, and each writes to its own keys. And for the keys it handles, `dot` is the sum over only 32 dimensions (either 0-31 or 32-63), not the full 64 dimensions. So we still need to combine the two halves for each key. For key_idx=0: lane 0 computes sum of dims 0-31, lane 32 computes sum of dims 32-63. We need to add them. Since key_idx=0 is only processed by lane 0 and lane 32, and key_idx=64 is processed by lane 0 and lane 32 on a later iteration... this gets complicated. Better approach: have all 64 threads in a head group iterate over the SAME key, compute partial dots, shuffle within 32, then combine the two warps using shared memory. For key_idx in 0..seq_len-1: - All 64 threads in head group load k[key_idx, :] for their assigned dim - Compute partial dot = q[dim] * k[key_idx, dim] - Shuffle within 32-thread warp - Write warp sum to shmem - One thread combines the two warp sums - Write final score to scores_shared[head][key_idx] This is sequential over keys but parallel over dimensions. Not ideal because we're not utilizing the parallel key iteration. But seq_len is only 511, and each dot product is 64 MACs. For 511 keys and 64 threads: 511 * 64 MACs = 32K MACs per block. At 67 TFLOPS, that's ~0.5 microseconds. But memory bound, it might take more like 5-10 microseconds. If we process keys sequentially, all 64 threads work on one key at a time. For 511 keys, that's 511 iterations. In each iteration, all threads read one float (k[key_idx, dim]). Memory access is good (coalesced, adjacent dims). But it's sequential. Alternatively, use the subwarp approach but process 2 keys per warp cycle. Actually, let me try yet another design: For each head group of 64 threads: - Subwarp 0 (32 threads): handles dims 0-31, computes dot products for ALL keys - Subwarp 1 (32 threads): handles dims 32-63, computes dot products for ALL keys - For key j, subwarp 0 computes dot0[j] = sum_{d=0..31} q[d]*k[j,d] - For key j, subwarp 1 computes dot1[j] = sum_{d=32..63} q[d]*k[j,d] - The total score[j] = dot0[j] + dot1[j] To compute this: - Subwarp 0 processes keys in parallel: thread t handles keys t, t+32, t+64, ... - For key j, thread t reads k[j, t] and computes q[t] * k[j, t] - Within subwarp, shuffle to get dot0[j] - Subwarp 1 similarly computes dot1[j] The challenge is still combining dot0 and dot1 for the same key j. If subwarp 0 writes dot0[j] to shared memory, and subwarp 1 writes dot1[j], then any thread can read both and sum them. But subwarp 0 and 1 process different sets of keys in their parallel loops. What if we have subwarp 0 loop over all keys, and for each key j, store dot0[j]. Similarly subwarp 1. After both finish, any thread can sum them for all j. We can allocate `float partial0[512]` and `float partial1[512]` in shared memory for each head. But 4 heads * 2 * 512 * 4 bytes = 16KB. That's feasible! Then the algorithm is: 1. Subwarp 0: for j = sublane; j < seq_len; j += 32: - Compute dot0[j] using shuffle - Write to shared_mem0[head][j] 2. Subwarp 1: similarly for dot1[j] 3. __syncthreads() 4. Any thread: for j in its keys, score[j] = (shared_mem0[head][j] + shared_mem1[head][j]) * scale For step 4, we can have each thread handle a subset of keys. This is much cleaner! Let me write it: ```cuda __shared__ float dot0_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float dot1_shared[4][512]; // Subwarp 0: lane < 32 if (lane < 32) { for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k_val = k[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; // Wait, q_val is q[d] where d = lane for subwarp 0 // But we're in the head group of 64 threads. Thread tid = head*64 + lane. // For subwarp 0, lane = 0..31 corresponds to dims 0..31. // For subwarp 1, lane = 32..63 corresponds to dims 32..63. #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (sublane == 0) { // Actually, all threads in subwarp have same dot after shuffle dot0_shared[head][j] = dot; } } } else { for (int j = lane - 32; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k_val = k[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } if (sublane == 0) { dot1_shared[head][j] = dot; } } } __syncthreads(); // Now compute actual scores for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 64) { scores_shared[head][j] = (dot0_shared[head][j] + dot1_shared[head][j]) * scale; } __syncthreads(); ``` Wait, I used `lane` as `tid % head_dim` which is 0..63. For subwarp 0: lane 0..31. They compute dot0 for keys j = lane, lane+32, lane+64, ... = 0, 32, 64, ...; 1, 33, 65, ...; etc. For subwarp 1: lane 32..63. They compute dot1 for keys j = lane-32, lane-32+32, ... = 0, 32, 64, ...; 1, 33, 65, ...; etc. So dot0_shared[head][j] and dot1_shared[head][j] are written for all j. After `__syncthreads()`, each lane 0..63 can read `scores_shared[head][j]` for j = lane, lane+64, ... and compute them from the shared memory dots. This works! The memory overhead is 4 * 512 * 2 * 4 = 16KB. Plus scores_shared = 4 * 512 * 4 = 8KB. Total 24KB shared memory. Well within limits. Actually, we don't even need separate dot0_shared and dot1_shared. We can just accumulate directly in scores_shared: ```cuda __shared__ float scores_shared[4][512]; // Subwarp 0 if (lane < 32) { for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k_val = k[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane] * k_val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); if (sublane == 0) scores_shared[head][j] = dot; } } else { for (int j = lane - 32; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k_val = k[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane] * k_val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); if (sublane == 0) scores_shared[head][j] = dot; } } __syncthreads(); // Now sum the two contributions for each j // Wait, this doesn't work because both subwarps wrote to the same scores_shared! // Subwarp 0 wrote the sum of dims 0-31, subwarp 1 wrote the sum of dims 32-63. // But they wrote to the SAME j's! So the second write overwrites the first. // We need two separate buffers. __shared__ float dot0_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float dot1_shared[4][512]; ``` Ah yes, they both wrote to `scores_shared[head][j]` for the same j values, leading to a race/overwrite. So we do need separate buffers. Or, we can have subwarp 0 write to `scores_shared[head][j]` and subwarp 1 write to `scores_shared[head][j]` ATOMICALLY ADD. But atomicAdd on shared memory is slower. So separate buffers it is. 24KB is fine. OK, let me now finalize the attention kernel with this corrected design. For the weighted sum, we can use a similar approach: - Each subwarp computes its partial weighted sum for its assigned keys - Store in shared memory - Sum them Or, since we process keys in parallel with each thread, and each thread handles its own keys, the full weighted sum for each dimension is in the thread's local variable. We just need to write it: ```cuda float out_val = 0.0f; for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 64) { float score = ...; float v_val = v[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; out_val += score * v_val; } attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane] = out_val; ``` Wait, lane is 0..63. So lane=0 handles keys 0, 64, 128, ...; lane=1 handles 1, 65, 129, ... Each lane computes the FULL weighted sum for its dimension because the score is the total attention score computed from all 64 dims. This is correct! No need for subwarp separation here because the score[j] is already the full score (we combined dot0 and dot1 for each j). After computing all scores and softmax, any thread can read any score[j] from shared memory. So each thread lane reads the scores for its assigned keys j = lane, lane+64, lane+128, ... and accumulates the weighted sum for its dimension. This is fully correct. Let me write the complete attention kernel now. For the reduction of `local_max` and `local_sum`, we need to find the max/sum across all lanes for each head. With 64 threads per head: - Warp 0 (lanes 0-31): reduce within warp using shuffle - Warp 1 (lanes 32-63): reduce within warp using shuffle - Store partial results in shared memory - Thread 0 combines them We already have `dot_partial[4][2]` for this. Here's the full kernel skeleton for attention: ```cuda __global__ void electra_attention_kernel(...) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int head = tid / 64; int lane = tid % 64; int sublane = lane % 32; if (token_idx >= seq_len || head >= num_heads || tid >= num_heads * head_dim) return; __shared__ float dot0_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float dot1_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float scores_shared[4][512]; __shared__ float attn_out_shared[256]; __shared__ float result_shared[256]; __shared__ float partial[4][2]; int head_offset = head * 64; // Load Q float q_val = q[token_idx * hidden + head_offset + lane]; // Compute dot products if (lane < 32) { for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k_val = k[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); if (sublane == 0) dot0_shared[head][j] = dot; } } else { for (int j = lane - 32; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float k_val = k[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; float dot = q_val * k_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); if (sublane == 0) dot1_shared[head][j] = dot; } } __syncthreads(); // Combine and compute scores if (lane < 32) { for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 32) { float total = dot0_shared[head][j] + dot1_shared[head][j]; scores_shared[head][j] = total * scale; } } __syncthreads(); // Find max score float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 64) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, scores_shared[head][j]); } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } __syncthreads(); if (sublane == 0) partial[head][lane / 32] = local_max; __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) { float m = partial[head][0]; if (partial[head][1] > m) m = partial[head][1]; partial[head][0] = m; } __syncthreads(); float max_score = partial[head][0]; // Compute sum of exp float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 64) { local_sum += expf(scores_shared[head][j] - max_score); } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } __syncthreads(); if (sublane == 0) partial[head][lane / 32] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); if (lane == 0) { partial[head][0] = partial[head][0] + partial[head][1]; } __syncthreads(); float sum_exp = partial[head][0]; float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Weighted sum float out_val = 0.0f; for (int j = lane; j < seq_len; j += 64) { float score = expf(scores_shared[head][j] - max_score) * inv_sum; float v_val = v[j * hidden + head_offset + lane]; out_val += score * v_val; } attn_out_shared[head_offset + lane] = out_val; __syncthreads(); // Output projection float proj = b_O[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { proj += attn_out_shared[i] * W_O_t[i * hidden + tid]; } // Add residual proj += residual_input[token_idx * hidden + tid]; result_shared[tid] = proj; __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm if (tid < 128) { // ... tree reduction on 256 elements } // Apply LN to all 256 dims if (tid < hidden) { output[token_idx * hidden + tid] = (result_shared[tid] - mean) * inv_std * ln_gamma[tid] + ln_beta[tid]; } } ``` Wait, `tid` ranges from 0..255. But I have the condition `tid >= num_heads * head_dim` for return. With 256 threads and num_heads=4, head_dim=64, `num_heads * head_dim = 256`. So `tid >= 256` is never true. All 256 threads are active. For the reduction, with 256 threads: ```cuda // Each thread loads one element float val = (tid < hidden) ? result_shared[tid] : 0.0f; __shared__ float sdata[256]; sdata[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 128) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 128]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 64) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 64]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 32]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 16]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 8]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 4]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 2]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 1]; } __syncthreads(); float mean = sdata[0] / hidden; ``` This works for 256 threads. Good. Now for the FFN kernel: ```cuda __global__ void electra_ffn_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* W_inter_t, const float* b_inter, const float* W_out_t, const float* b_out, const float* ln_gamma, const float* ln_beta, int seq_len, int hidden, int intermediate_size ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (token_idx >= seq_len || tid >= intermediate_size) return; __shared__ float inter_shared[1024]; __shared__ float result_shared[256]; __shared__ float sdata[256]; // Load input into shared memory if (tid < hidden) { sdata[tid] = input[token_idx * hidden + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute intermediate: tid computes one output if tid < inter_size // inter_size = 1024, block = 256 threads, each thread computes 4 outputs if (tid < intermediate_size) { float sum = b_inter[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { sum += sdata[i] * W_inter_t[i * intermediate_size + tid]; } // GELU sum = 0.5f * sum * (1.0f + erff(sum * 0.70710678118f)); inter_shared[tid] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute output projection: 256 outputs, 256 threads, each computes 1 output if (tid < hidden) { float sum = b_out[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < intermediate_size; i++) { sum += inter_shared[i] * W_out_t[i * hidden + tid]; } sum += sdata[tid]; // residual result_shared[tid] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm // ... same reduction as before } ``` Wait, the block size is 256 threads, but intermediate_size is 1024. For intermediate computation, 256 threads each computing 4 outputs: `tid` from 0..255 computes outputs `tid`, `tid+256`, `tid+512`, `tid+768`? No, that strided access pattern for weights is non-coalesced. Better: have 256 threads where thread `tid` computes a contiguous chunk. But 1024 / 256 = 4. So thread `tid` computes outputs `tid*4`, `tid*4+1`, `tid*4+2`, `tid*4+3`. This requires reading weight columns in strided fashion. Actually, for coalescing, we should have thread `tid` read `W_inter_t[i * intermediate_size + tid]` for its first output. If we store `W_inter_t` transposed as `[intermediate_size, hidden]` -> wait, no. In the previous kernels, I transposed the weight matrices so that thread `tid` reads `W_t[k * N + tid]` (for output `tid` and input `k`). This is coalesced because adjacent threads read adjacent floats. For FFN1, if we transpose `W_intermediate` to `W_inter_t` of shape `[intermediate_size, hidden]`? Wait, the original `W_intermediate` is `[hidden, intermediate_size] = [256, 1024]`. Transposed, it becomes `[1024, 256]`. Then for output `j`, we read `W_inter_t[j * hidden + i]` for varying `i`. If thread `tid` computes output `tid` (and maybe `tid+256`, etc.), then for a fixed `i`, adjacent threads read: - Thread 0: W_inter_t[0*256 + i] - Thread 1: W_inter_t[1*256 + i] - ... For fixed i and varying j, W_inter_t[j * 256 + i] is strided by 256 floats = 1024 bytes. Not coalesced! To get coalescing, we need to store W_inter_t such that thread `tid` reads adjacent memory. If we store W_inter_t as `W.transpose(0,1).contiguous()`, shape is [1024, 256]. Then W_inter_t[j, i] for adjacent j are adjacent in memory (stride 256 * 4 = 1024 bytes within j, but j varies). Wait, no: Row-major: W_inter_t[j * 256 + i] For fixed i=0, varying j: - j=0: index 0 - j=1: index 256 - j=2: index 512 These are 1024 bytes apart. For coalescing, adjacent threads should read adjacent bytes. So if thread 0 reads index 0, thread 1 should read index 1, not index 256. We want thread `tid` to read W_inter_t[k, tid] for fixed k. But then adjacent threads read adjacent memory: - Thread 0: W_inter_t[k * 256 + 0] - Thread 1: W_inter_t[k * 256 + 1] Yes! These are adjacent floats. So for output j computed by thread tid, the access is W_inter_t[k * 256 + tid]? Wait, if thread tid computes output j, and j might not equal tid. What if thread tid computes output j = tid? Then it reads W_inter_t[k * hidden + tid] for k = 0..255. For fixed k, adjacent threads read adjacent columns. Good! But then thread 0 computes output 0, thread 1 computes output 1, etc. For intermediate_size=1024, we'd need 1024 threads. We only have 256. What if each thread computes 4 outputs: j = tid, tid+256, tid+512, tid+768? - For k=0: thread 0 reads W_inter_t[0*256 + 0], thread 1 reads W_inter_t[1*256 + 1]? No, thread tid computes output j, so it reads W_inter_t[k * 256 + j] for varying k. Wait, I defined W_inter_t as W.transpose(0,1).contiguous(), so shape is [1024, 256]. For computing output j (where j is the output index), the dot product is: `output[j] = sum_k input[k] * W_inter_t[j * 256 + k]` No, the original W is [256, 1024], meaning W[k, j] connects input k to output j. Transposed W_t is [1024, 256], W_t[j, k] = W[k, j]. So `output[j] = sum_k input[k] * W_t[j * 256 + k]`. Thread tid computing output j reads W_t[j * 256 + k] for k = 0..255. For k=0: thread tid reads W_t[j * 256 + 0]. If thread 0 computes j=0, it reads W_t[0]. If thread 1 computes j=1, it reads W_t[256]. These are NOT adjacent. But if thread 0 computes j=0, and thread 1 ALSO computes j=0 but for a different k? No, we want each thread to compute a different output. For coalescing, we need threads in a warp to read adjacent memory. So for a fixed k, threads should read W_t[j*256 + k] for adjacent j. But each thread only reads for one j. Oh wait, if we process k in an outer loop: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float x = input[k]; // broadcast for (int j_idx = 0; j_idx < 4; j_idx++) { int j = tid + j_idx * 256; out[j_idx] += x * W_t[j * hidden + k]; } } ``` For fixed k and j_idx=0: thread tid reads W_t[tid * hidden + k]. Adjacent threads read W_t[0*256+k], W_t[1*256+k], ... These are 256 floats apart. NOT coalesced. For coalescing, we need W_t stored so that W_t[k, j] for adjacent j are adjacent. That means W_t should have shape [hidden, intermediate_size] with inner dimension being intermediate_size. But that's the original W, not transposed! Original W: [hidden=256, intermediate_size=1024], stored row-major. W[k, j] at index k * 1024 + j. For fixed k=0, adjacent j are adjacent in memory. Thread tid computing output j reads W[k * 1024 + j]. If thread 0 computes j=0, it reads W[0]. Thread 1 computes j=1, it reads W[1]. These ARE adjacent and coalesced! But thread tid computes output j=tid. In the inner loop over k: ```cuda for (int k = 0; k < hidden; k++) { float x = input_shared[k]; out += x * W[k * intermediate_size + tid]; } ``` For fixed k=0: threads read W[0], W[1], W[2], ... adjacent, coalesced. But as k increases, the next read is W[1024 + tid], then W[2048 + tid], etc. Adjacent threads still read adjacent memory within each k iteration. So storing weights in the ORIGINAL row-major format [256, 1024] gives coalescing! Wait, this is crucial. For GEMV where outputs are distributed across threads, and we iterate over input dimension with each thread reading a fixed output column, the weights should be stored as [K, N] so that adjacent outputs are adjacent in memory. For QKV where N=256, K=256: - Original W_Q: [256, 256] - Thread tid computes output j=tid - Reads W_Q[k * 256 + tid] for k=0..255 - Coalesced! For FFN1 where N=1024, K=256: - Original W_inter: [256, 1024] - Thread tid computes outputs j=tid, tid+256, tid+512, tid+768 - For k=0: - Reads W_inter[0*1024 + tid], W_inter[0*1024 + tid+256], etc. - Adjacent threads read tid, tid+1 which are adjacent. Coalesced! - As k increases, reads W_inter[k*1024 + tid], etc. So we DON'T need to transpose! The original PyTorch weight format works perfectly for this access pattern. This simplifies things significantly. I don't need to transpose any weights. For FFN2: W_out = [1024, 256] (original [intermediate_size, hidden]). Thread tid computes output j=tid. Reads W_out[k * 256 + tid] for k=0..1023. Coalesced! So I'll pass weights in their original format. Now let me rewrite the kernels with this insight. **Corrected QKV kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void electra_qkv_kernel( const float* input, float* q, float* k, float* v, const float* W_Q, const float* b_Q, const float* W_K, const float* b_K, const float* W_V, const float* b_V, int seq_len, int hidden ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; __shared__ float x_shared[256]; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; if (tid < hidden) { x_shared[tid] = input[token_idx * hidden + tid]; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < hidden) { float q_sum = b_Q[tid]; float k_sum = b_K[tid]; float v_sum = b_V[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { float val = x_shared[i]; q_sum += val * W_Q[i * hidden + tid]; k_sum += val * W_K[i * hidden + tid]; v_sum += val * W_V[i * hidden + tid]; } q[token_idx * hidden + tid] = q_sum; k[token_idx * hidden + tid] = k_sum; v[token_idx * hidden + tid] = v_sum; } } ``` For the attention kernel, the output projection weight W_O has shape [hidden, hidden] = [256, 256]. **FFN kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void electra_ffn_kernel( const float* input, float* output, const float* W_inter, const float* b_inter, const float* W_out, const float* b_out, const float* ln_gamma, const float* ln_beta, int seq_len, int hidden, int intermediate_size ) { int token_idx = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (token_idx >= seq_len) return; __shared__ float input_shared[256]; __shared__ float inter_shared[1024]; __shared__ float result_shared[256]; __shared__ float sdata[256]; if (tid < hidden) { input_shared[tid] = input[token_idx * hidden + tid]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute intermediate: 1024 outputs. 256 threads, each computes 4 outputs. // tid computes outputs: tid, tid+256, tid+512, tid+768 float inter_vals[4]; for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { int out_idx = tid + j * 256; float sum = b_inter[out_idx]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { sum += input_shared[i] * W_inter[i * intermediate_size + out_idx]; } // GELU exact sum = 0.5f * sum * (1.0f + erff(sum * 0.70710678118f)); inter_shared[out_idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute output projection: 256 outputs. 256 threads, each computes 1 output. if (tid < hidden) { float sum = b_out[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < intermediate_size; sdata[tid] = mean_val; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 128) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 128]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 64) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 64]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 32]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 16]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 8]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 4]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 2]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 1]; } __syncthreads(); float mean = sdata[0] / hidden; float var_val = (tid < hidden) ? (result_shared[tid] - mean) * (result_shared[tid] - mean) : 0.0f; sdata[tid] = var_val; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 128) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 128]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 64) sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 64]; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 32]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 16]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 8]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 4]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 2]; sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + 1]; } __syncthreads(); float var = sdata[0] / hidden; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-12f); if (tid < hidden) { output[token_idx * hidden + tid] = (result_shared[tid] - mean) * inv_std * ln_gamma[tid] + ln_beta[tid]; } } ``` Wait, the intermediate computation with 256 threads each doing 4 outputs has a problem: each thread writes to `inter_shared[tid]`, `inter_shared[tid+256]`, etc. All threads in a warp write to memory locations that are 256 apart. This doesn't cause bank conflicts in shared memory because they're in different banks. But for the output projection, when all threads read `inter_shared[i]` for fixed i, there's a broadcast (no bank conflict). This is fine. Actually, for the intermediate computation, the weight reads are: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { sum += input_shared[i] * W_inter[i * intermediate_size + out_idx]; } ``` Thread `tid` reads `W_inter[i * 1024 + out_idx]` for its `out_idx`. With `out_idx = tid + j*256`, adjacent threads read adjacent columns: `i*1024 + tid`, `i*1024 + tid+1`, etc. These are coalesced because adjacent threads read adjacent memory. Good! But what about `j` loop? Thread tid does 4 separate outputs, reading 4 separate columns for each `i`. These reads are strided by 256 floats = 1024 bytes. For a single thread, reading 4 strided columns in sequence might be okay due to cache lines. But it's not ideal. An alternative: have each warp compute a contiguous chunk of outputs. - Warp 0 (threads 0-31): outputs 0..31 - Warp 1 (threads 32-63): outputs 32..63 - ... - Warp 7 (threads 224-255): outputs 224..255 This gives 8 warps handling 32 outputs each. But intermediate_size is 1024. So we'd need 32 warps! Or we can process the intermediate in tiles. But for simplicity, the 256-thread approach computing 4 outputs each is fine. Actually, let me double-check: with `out_idx = tid + j * 256`, for j=0, out_idx ranges from 0..255 across threads. For j=1, out_idx ranges from 256..511. So each j iteration handles a full block of 256 contiguous outputs. That's great! The warp processes contiguous outputs for a given j. But wait, `for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++)` loops over j, computing one block of 256 outputs at a time. All 256 threads participate for each j. This is perfect! The code: ```cuda for (int j = 0; j < 4; j++) { int out_idx = tid + j * 256; float sum = b_inter[out_idx]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { sum += input_shared[i] * W_inter[i * intermediate_size + out_idx]; } inter_shared[out_idx] = sum; } ``` For j=0: all threads compute outputs 0..255. Adjacent threads read adjacent weights: `W_inter[i*1024 + tid]`. Coalesced. For j=1: all threads compute outputs 256..511. Adjacent threads read `W_inter[i*1024 + tid + 256]`. Coalesced. And so on. This is actually quite efficient because each j iteration uses all threads on a contiguous block. However, there are 4 iterations, and `sum` and `out_idx` change. This can be unrolled for performance. OK, I think the FFN kernel is workable. Now for the attention kernel, let me also fix the output projection at the end: ```cuda // Output projection float proj = b_O[tid]; for (int i = 0; i < hidden; i++) { proj += attn_out_shared[i] * W_O[i * hidden + tid]; } ``` With W_O shape [256, 256], `W_O[i * 256 + tid]`. Adjacent threads read adjacent columns. Coalesced. Good. And for the embeddings projection: ```cuda sum += x_shared[i] * proj_weight[i * hidden_dim + tid]; ``` With proj_weight shape [128, 256], `proj_weight[i * 256 + tid]`. Adjacent threads read adjacent memory. Coalesced. Good. Now I need to handle the `ln_gamma` and `ln_beta` shapes. For attention output LN, they have shape [256]. For FFN output LN, also [256]. One more thing: `erff` is a CUDA device function for `erf(float)`. And `M_SQRT1_2` is `0.70710678118`. We can also just use `0.70710678118f`. For exact GELU: `0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118f))` Wait, let me verify the exact GELU formula in PyTorch: ```python def gelu(x): return 0.5 * x * (1 + torch.erf(x / sqrt(2))) ``` So: `0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x / sqrtf(2.0f)))` Or equivalently `0.5f * x * (1.0f + erff(x * 0.70710678118f))`. Let me use the exact value to match PyTorch as closely as possible. Now, let me think about whether we need dropout. As I discussed, I'll assume eval mode and skip dropout. **LM Head:** The reference might use `self.model.generator_lm_head`. Its weight shape could be [30522, 256] or [30522, 128]. I'll check at initialization and handle accordingly. If the model actually works (and I believe it does in the evaluation environment), then the LM head must accept the hidden_size input. So its weight shape should be [vocab_size, hidden_size] or [hidden_size, vocab_size]. In PyTorch `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)`, `weight` has shape `[out_features, in_features]`. So if `generator_lm_head` is `nn.Linear(hidden_size, vocab_size)`, weight shape is `[30522, 256]`. If it's `nn.Linear(embedding_size, vocab_size)`, weight shape is `[30522, 128]`. Since the input to the LM head is `sequence_output` of shape [seq, hidden_size] = [seq, 256], it must be `nn.Linear(256, 30522)` or there must be a linear projection. Let me just handle it in Python: ```python lm_head = self.model.generator_lm_head lm_weight = lm_head.weight lm_bias = lm_head.bias ``` If `lm_weight.shape[1] != hidden_size`, I need to figure out what to do. But I'll add a projection if needed. Actually, I'll write the Python code to work generically. **Constructing ModelNew:** ```python class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.model_name = model_name self.config = config # Load reference model to extract weights self.ref_model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) self.ref_model.eval() # Extract embedding weights embeddings = self.ref_model.electra.embeddings # ... # Extract layer weights layers = self.ref_model.electra.encoder.layer # ... def forward(self, x): # Call custom CUDA kernels # ... return logits ``` For the forward pass, I need to manage intermediate buffers. Since we launch many kernels, I need to allocate GPU buffers and ping-pong between them. For batch=1, seq=511, hidden=256: - Buffer size: 511 * 256 * 4 = ~512KB - We need a few buffers Let me allocate: - `scratch1`: [seq, hidden] - `scratch2`: [seq, hidden] - `q`: [seq, hidden] - `k`: [seq, hidden] - `v`: [seq, hidden] Actually, QKV are computed and consumed before the next layer, so we can reuse them across layers. But in our kernel design, the qkv_kernel writes Q, K, V, then the attention_kernel reads them and writes output. Then the ffn_kernel reads output and writes final_output. For layer i: - Input: buffer1 - qkv_kernel writes q, k, v - attention_kernel reads q, k, v and buffer1 (residual), writes buffer2 - ffn_kernel reads buffer2, writes buffer1 So we ping-pong between buffer1 and buffer2. For the next layer: - Input: buffer1 - ... - FFN writes buffer2 We swap the roles each layer. Since ELECTRA has 12 layers (even), the final output ends up in buffer1. For the LM head: - logits = buffer1 @ lm_weight.T + lm_bias Now let me write the Python code for `forward`: ```python def forward(self, x): # x shape: [batch, seq] batch_size, seq_len = x.shape assert batch_size == 1 # Call embedding kernel self.electra_embeddings_forward(x, self.scratch1) # Ping-pong buffers current = self.scratch1 next_buf = self.scratch2 # For each layer for i in range(self.num_layers): self.electra_qkv_forward(current, self.q_buf, self.k_buf, self.v_buf, i) self.electra_attention_forward(self.q_buf, self.k_buf, self.v_buf, current, next_buf, i) self.electra_ffn_forward(next_buf, current, i) # After 12 layers (even number), output is in current (scratch1) # LM head logits = torch.matmul(current, self.lm_weight.t()) + self.lm_bias return logits ``` Wait, the FFN kernel writes output to a buffer, but I named the arguments carefully: - `electra_ffn_forward(input, output, layer_idx)` - So if `input=next_buf` and `output=current`, after FFN, `current` has the result. For the next layer, we use `current` as input. Good. But for the attention kernel, we need to pass the residual (original input to the layer). So: - `electra_attention_forward(q, k, v, residual_input, output, layer_idx)` - `residual_input` is `current` before attention - `output` is `next_buf` after attention For LM head, I should use `torch.nn.functional.linear(current, self.lm_weight, self.lm_bias)` or `torch.matmul`. Actually, the shape should be [batch, seq, hidden] @ [hidden, vocab_size] -> [batch, seq, vocab_size]. In PyTorch, `self.lm_weight` is [vocab_size, hidden] for `nn.Linear`. So: `torch.matmul(current, self.lm_weight.t()) + self.lm_bias` Where `current` is [1, 511, 256] (reshaped from [511, 256]), `lm_weight.t()` is [256, 30522]. Wait, in my kernel, the buffers are [seq, hidden] = [511, 256]. For `torch.matmul`, I need [1, 511, 256]. So I can reshape: `current.view(1, seq_len, -1)`. Actually, `torch.matmul` can also handle 2D @ 2D, giving [511, 30522]. Then I need to add batch dimension: `[1, 511, 30522]`. Yes: ```python logits_2d = torch.matmul(current, self.lm_weight.t()) + self.lm_bias logits = logits_2d.unsqueeze(0) # [1, seq, vocab] ``` Now, for the CUDA kernels, I need to pass individual weight tensors for each layer. I can pack them as lists and index them in Python, passing the specific tensors for the layer index. This means my C++ function needs to accept `at::Tensor` for layer-specific weights. With `load_inline`, we can write host wrapper functions that accept tensors. The function signature in C++ would be: ```cpp void electra_qkv_forward( at::Tensor input, at::Tensor q, at::Tensor k, at::Tensor v, at::Tensor W_Q, at::Tensor b_Q, at::Tensor W_K, at::Tensor b_K, at::Tensor W_V, at::Tensor b_V ) ``` And similar for other kernels. In the `cpp_sources` of `load_inline`, we write: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> // CUDA kernels declarations __global__ void electra_embeddings_kernel(...); __global__ void electra_qkv_kernel(...); __global__ void electra_attention_kernel(...); __global__ void electra_ffn_kernel(...); // Wrapper functions void electra_embeddings_forward(at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor output, ...) { // Launch kernel } void electra_qkv_forward(at::Tensor input, at::Tensor q, ...) { int seq_len = input.size(0); int hidden = input.size(1); electra_qkv_kernel<<<seq_len, 256>>>(...); } // etc. ``` And in Python: ```python functions = ['electra_embeddings_forward', 'electra_qkv_forward', 'electra_attention_forward', 'electra_ffn_forward'] ``` Now, there's an important detail about ATen tensors in CUDA kernels. We need to get raw pointers: ```cpp float* input_ptr = input.data_ptr<float>(); ``` And we need to make sure tensors are contiguous and on CUDA. In `__init__`, after extracting weights, I'll move them to CUDA and make them contiguous. One more thing: `seq_len` is 511, which is not a multiple of block size. In my kernels, `gridDim.x = seq_len`, so we launch 511 blocks. Each kernel has the check `if (token_idx >= seq_len) return;` which is unnecessary since `token_idx = blockIdx.x` and gridDim.x = seq_len. But it's harmless. Actually, the `seq_len` parameter in the kernel is only needed for loops inside the attention kernel. `blockIdx.x` ranges from 0 to 510. Good. Wait, is 511 blocks enough to saturate H100? Probably not fully, but it's small enough that each block does substantial work, and H100 is very fast. Let me reconsider the grid size. For the attention kernel, each block handles one token.
